Compute H = P + t v = p_u u + (p_v + t) v. Then H·PN = (p_u u + (p_v + t) v)·((d - p_u) u - p_v v) = p_u (d - p_u) (u·u) + (p_v + t) (-p_v) (v·v) + cross terms zero (u·v=0). So = p_u (d - p_u) - p_v (p_v + t). Set = 0:

p_u (d - p_u) - p_v (p_v + t) = 0 → p_u (d - p_u) = p_v (p_v + t) → p_v (p_v + t) = p_u (d - p_u). Solve for t:

p_v^2 + p_v t = p_u (d - p_u) → p_v t = p_u (d - p_u) - p_v^2 → t = (p_u (d - p_u) - p_v^2) / p_v.

Then H = p_u u + (p_v + t) v = p_u u + (p_v + (p_u (d - p_u) - p_v^2)/p_v) v = p_u u + ( (p_v^2 + p_u (d - p_u) - p_v^2) / p_v ) v = p_u u + ( p_u (d - p_u) / p_v ) v.

Thus H = (p_u, p_u (d - p_u) / p_v) in coordinates (u,v). So indeed H = (p_u, p_u(d-p_u)/p_v). That's a nice expression.

Let's compute MP^2 in terms of r and R. Simplify the bracket:

B = 1 + (x0 + r)^2 / (r^2 - x0^2). Note that r^2 - x0^2 = (r - x0)(r + x0). Also (x0 + r)^2 = (r + x0)^2. So:

B = 1 + (r + x0)^2 / ((r - x0)(r + x0)) = 1 + (r + x0) / (r - x0). Because (r + x0)^2 / ((r - x0)(r + x0)) = (r + x0) / (r - x0). So:

MP^2 = p_u^2 * [1 + (r + x0) / (r - x0)] = p_u^2 * [(r - x0) + (r + x0)] / (r - x0) = p_u^2 * (2r) / (r - x0).

Thus MP^2 = p_u^2 * (2r) / (r - x0). Now we also have relation between x0, r, R. Since x0 = (1 + r^2 - R^2)/2, then r - x0 = r - (1 + r^2 - R^2)/2 = (2r - 1 - r^2 + R^2)/2 = (R^2 - r^2 + 2r - 1)/2. That seems not obviously something like (something). But maybe we can compute p_u^2 * (2r) / (r - x0) and simplify to r^2 (i.e., MP^2 = r^2). Let's try.

We have p_u = (1 + R - r)/2. Then p_u^2 = ( (1 + R - r)^2 ) / 4.

Now compute r - x0. x0 = (1 + r^2 - R^2)/2. So:

r - x0 = r - (1 + r^2 - R^2)/2 = (2r - 1 - r^2 + R^2) / 2.

Now MP^2 = p_u^2 * (2r) / (r - x0) = ((1 + R - r)^2 / 4) * (2r) / ((2r - 1 - r^2 + R^2)/2) = ((1 + R - r)^2 / 4) * (2r) * (2 / (2r - 1 - r^2 + R^2)) = ((1 + R - r)^2 / 4) * (4r) / (2r - 1 - r^2 + R^2) = ((1 + R - r)^2 * r) / (2r - 1 - r^2 + R^2).

So MP^2 = r * (1 + R - r)^2 / (2r - 1 - r^2 + R^2). That's not obviously r^2. So |t_C| = r / d, where d = MN. Since t_C is negative, t_C = -r / d. So C = M - (r / d) * (N - M) = M - (r / d) * (N - M). Similarly, D is on Γ such that N is between M and D, so D is on the ray beyond N from M, with t_D > 1. D = N + (R / d) * (N - M) (since ND = R). So D = N + (R / d) * (N - M). That's consistent.

So we can compute C and D in terms of M,N,r,R.

Now, P is circumcenter of ACD. Since A is origin, we can compute P as the intersection of perpendicular bisectors. Perhaps we can find an expression for P using vector formulas. For triangle with vertices A=0, C, D. The circumcenter is the point P such that |P|^2 = |P - C|^2 = |P - D|^2. So:

|P|^2 = |P - C|^2 → |P|^2 = |P|^2 - 2 P·C + |C|^2 → 2 P·C = |C|^2. Similarly, 2 P·D = |D|^2. So P satisfies:

P·C = |C|^2 / 2, P·D = |D|^2 / 2.

Thus P is the solution to these linear equations. Since C and D are not collinear (they are not parallel generally), there is a unique P. This is a nice expression. (h_x - m_x, h_y - m_y) · (n_x - p, n_y - 0) = 0 → (h_x - m_x)*(n_x - p) + (h_y - m_y)*n_y = 0.   (B)

We can also use the fact that H is the orthocenter, so H = P + M + N - 2 O, but O can be computed using perpendicular bisectors. But maybe we can find a simpler expression: Since P is on x-axis, we might consider the altitudes. The altitude from P is perpendicular to MN. So H lies on the line through P perpendicular to MN. That line is not necessarily vertical. But we have H also lies on altitude from M, which is perpendicular to PN. So that's messy.

But perhaps we can use vector algebra to derive H_y in terms of m_x, n_x, m_y, n_y, p. Let's attempt.

We have unknowns h_x, h_y. Equations:

(1) (h_x - p)*(m_x - n_x) + h_y*(m_y - n_y) = 0.
(2) (h_x - m_x)*(n_x - p) + (h_y - m_y)*n_y = 0.

We can solve for h_x and h_y. However, these are linear equations? They involve products like (h_x - p)*(m_x - n_x). That's linear in h_x, h_y. So they are linear equations. Indeed, (1) is: (m_x - n_x) h_x + (m_y - n_y) h_y = (m_x - n_x) p. That's linear. (2) expands: (n_x - p) h_x + n_y h_y = (n_x - p) m_x + n_y m_y. That's also linear. So we can solve.

Thus, H is determined by solving two linear equations. So we can compute h_x and h_y.

Now, also we have E = (2 m_x, 0), F = (2 n_x, 0). So we can compute O = (m_x + n_x, y0) where y0 is solved from |O - B|^2 = |O - E|^2. But B is unknown. However, we can express B in terms of M,N,r,R. Since A=0 is an intersection, we can solve for B. Let's do that.

We have:

|M| = r, |N| = R. Also, B lies on both circles, so |B - M| = r, |B - N| = R. Since A=0 also satisfies those. So B is the second intersection. The line MN is the perpendicular bisector of AB. So B is the reflection of A across the line MN? No, reflecting across a line gives a point such that the line is the perpendicular bisector of the segment connecting the point and its reflection. Indeed, if you reflect a point across a line, the line is the perpendicular bisector of the segment connecting the point and its image. So if MN is the perpendicular bisector of AB, then B is the reflection of A across the line MN. Since A is origin, B is the reflection of the origin across the line MN. That is a nice characterization. So B is the point obtained by reflecting the origin across the line MN. That's a linear transformation (reflection). So we can compute B as:

B = 2 * projection of origin onto line MN? Actually reflecting a point across a line: If line L is given by a point X0 and a direction vector v, then the reflection of point P across L is: P' = P + 2 * (proj_{n}(P - X0))? Let's derive. For a line through the origin, reflection is simply 2 * projection onto the line? Actually reflection across a line through the origin: if line L passes through origin with unit direction u, then reflection of a vector x is 2*(x·u) u - x. That's the reflection across L. But here MN is a line not necessarily through the origin. So we need to reflect the origin across that line. That's a bit different. The reflection of the origin across a line L (not through origin) is twice the foot of perpendicular from origin to L. Indeed, if L is a line, then the reflection of a point P across L is P' = P + 2 * (proj_{n}(P - X0))? Actually we can compute: Let F be the foot of perpendicular from origin to L. Then the reflection of origin across L is 2F. So B = 2F, where F is the foot of perpendicular from origin to line MN. So B is twice the foot. So we can compute F using projection.

Thus, B = 2 * foot, where foot is the orthogonal projection of origin onto line MN. So B is determined by M and N.

Now, we also have that M and N are on MN. So we can compute the foot. But perhaps we can find a direct relationship: Since MN is the perpendicular bisector of AB, and A=0, then B is such that for any point X on MN, XA = XB. In particular, for X = M and N, we already have MA = MB, NA = NB. That's consistent. So B is the reflection of A across MN. So indeed, B = reflect(A) across MN. Since A is origin, we have B = 2 * proj_{MN}(0) (foot)?? Actually reflecting the origin across a line L yields a point that is twice the foot of perpendicular from origin to L. That's correct. So B = 2 * foot.

Now, foot is the point on L such that the vector from foot to origin is perpendicular to L. So foot is the orthogonal projection of origin onto L. So foot = ( (0) - (point on L) ) projected? Let's compute.

Let L be the line passing through M and N. Parameter: M + t*(N-M). The foot of perpendicular from origin to L is the point F such that (F) is orthogonal to direction (N-M) and F lies on L. So we need to find t such that (M + t*(N-M)) is perpendicular to (N-M). That is, (M + t*(N-M)) · (N-M) = 0. Then F = M + t*(N-M). Then B = 2F.

So we can compute F.

Now, we also have C and D on MN. They are given as:

C = M - (r/d)*(N-M) (since C is opposite M from N). D = N + (R/d)*(N-M). So C and D are also points on L.

Now, P is the circumcenter of ACD. Since A=0, we have:

P·C = |C|^2/2, P·D = |D|^2/2. As before. And P lies on x-axis. So we can find p.

Now, H is orthocenter of PMN. So we need to relate H_y to the geometry. Perhaps we can find an expression for H_y in terms of M,N, and p. Let's attempt to compute H_y from the linear equations.

We have:

Equation (1): (m_x - n_x) h_x + (m_y - n_y) h_y = (m_x - n_x) p.   (1)

Equation (2): (n_x - p) h_x + n_y h_y = (n_x - p) m_x + n_y m_y.   (2)

We can solve for h_y.

Similarly, we can find y0 (the y-coordinate of the center O of circle BEF). Since O = (m_x + n_x, y0). The condition that O is equidistant to B and E gives:

|O - B|^2 = |O - E|^2. So:

(m_x + n_x - B_x)^2 + (y0 - B_y)^2 = (m_x + n_x - 2 m_x)^2 + (y0 - 0)^2 = (n_x - m_x)^2 + y0^2.

Thus:

(m_x + n_x - B_x)^2 + (y0 - B_y)^2 = (n_x - m_x)^2 + y0^2.

Expand:

(m_x + n_x - B_x)^2 + y0^2 - 2 y0 B_y + B_y^2 = (n_x - m_x)^2 + y0^2.

Cancel y0^2:

(m_x + n_x - B_x)^2 - 2 y0 B_y + B_y^2 = (n_x - m_x)^2.

Thus:

- 2 y0 B_y = (n_x - m_x)^2 - (m_x + n_x - B_x)^2 - B_y^2.

So we can solve for y0:

y0 = [ (m_x + n_x - B_x)^2 + B_y^2 - (n_x - m_x)^2 ] / (2 B_y), provided B_y ≠ 0. B_y is the y-coordinate of B. Since B is the second intersection and not on MN generally, B_y is non-zero (unless circles are tangent). So that's okay.

Now, also from the condition that MN is perpendicular bisector of AB, we can find B in terms of M,N. Let's compute B.

Let’s denote direction vector d = N - M. Then line L: M + t d. The foot F of perpendicular from origin to L is given by:

F = M + t0 d, where t0 satisfies (M + t0 d) · d = 0? Actually we want the foot from origin, so the vector from foot to origin (i.e., -F) is perpendicular to L. So F is such that F ⟂ d (since F is the point on L, and the line from F to origin is perpendicular to L). So (F) · d = 0? Because F is the vector from origin to foot, and it should be perpendicular to direction d. Indeed, if we have a line L with direction d, then the foot of perpendicular from origin to L is the point F on L such that (F) is orthogonal to d. So F · d = 0. That's correct. So we need to find t0 such that (M + t0 d) · d = 0 → M·d + t0 |d|^2 = 0 → t0 = - (M·d) / |d|^2.

Thus F = M - (M·d) / |d|^2 * d. Then B = 2F.

So B = 2M - 2 (M·d) / |d|^2 * d.

We can also express in terms of N and M. Since d = N - M.

Now, we have M and N coordinates unknown. But we can try to simplify the expression for y0.

Let's compute B_x and B_y. Since we have coordinates, we can compute y0. Then we also have H_y. Then the tangency condition becomes:

|y0 - H_y| = radius = sqrt( (n_x - m_x)^2 + y0^2 ). That's a specific equation. We can attempt to prove that (y0 - H_y)^2 = (n_x - m_x)^2 + y0^2. That simplifies to:

(y0 - H_y)^2 - y0^2 = (n_x - m_x)^2 → (y0^2 - 2 y0 H_y + H_y^2) - y0^2 = -2 y0 H_y + H_y^2 = (n_x - m_x)^2.

Thus:

H_y^2 - 2 y0 H_y = (n_x - m_x)^2.   (Eq T)

We can also derive a similar equation from the radius expression: radius^2 = (n_x - m_x)^2 + y0^2. That's what we used.

Given the complexity, perhaps we can find that y0 = -p? Or something like that. Let's test with numeric with A=0 transformed. In the numeric we had M=(0,0), N=(1,0), A=(0.28,0.96). Thus, I'll adopt the following coordinate system:

- Place A at the origin (0,0).
- Let the line AP be the x-axis. Since AP is a line, we can rotate so that AP is horizontal. So P lies on the x-axis: P = (p,0) with p ≠ 0.
- The points E and F are the second intersections of line AP (x-axis) with Ω and Γ respectively. As derived, with A=0, we have E = (2 m_x, 0) and F = (2 n_x, 0), where M = (m_x, m_y) and N = (n_x, n_y) are the centers. (But careful: This derivation used that |M| = r and |E - M| = r. However, is it guaranteed that E lies on the same line AP as A and P? Yes, by definition, line AP meets Ω again at E ≠ A, so E is on AP. So E is on the x-axis. So we have E = (e, 0). The condition |E - M| = r gives (e - m_x)^2 + m_y^2 = r^2. Since |M|^2 = r^2, we have:

(e - m_x)^2 + m_y^2 = m_x^2 + m_y^2 → e^2 - 2e m_x + m_x^2 + m_y^2 = m_x^2 + m_y^2 → e^2 - 2e m_x = 0 → e(e - 2 m_x) = 0. Since e ≠ 0 (E ≠ A), we get e = 2 m_x. So E = (2 m_x, 0). Good. Similarly, F = (2 n_x, 0). So indeed, E and F are determined by the x-coordinates of M and N.

Now, note that M and N are centers, so they are points with distances r and R from A (origin). So |M| = r, |N| = R. That gives:

m_x^2 + m_y^2 = r^2, n_x^2 + n_y^2 = R^2.

And P = (p,0) lies on the x-axis. So we have:

Thus, we can get expressions for C_x and D_x in terms of p. But also C and D are on MN, so they satisfy collinearity. This will give relationships.

|O - B|^2 = |O - E|^2.

Thus:

(m_x + n_x - B_x)^2 + (y0 - B_y)^2 = (m_x + n_x - 2 m_x)^2 + (y0 - 0)^2 = (n_x - m_x)^2 + y0^2.

(m_x + n_x - B_x)^2 + (y0 - B_y)^2 = (n_x - m_x)^2 + y0^2.

Expand (y0 - B_y)^2 = y0^2 - 2 y0 B_y + B_y^2. So:

(m_x + n_x - B_x)^2 + y0^2 - 2 y0 B_y + B_y^2 = (n_x - m_x)^2 + y0^2.

Cancel y0^2:

(m_x + n_x - B_x)^2 - 2 y0 B_y + B_y^2 = (n_x - m_x)^2.

Thus:

-2 y0 B_y = (n_x - m_x)^2 - (m_x + n_x - B_x)^2 - B_y^2.

So:

y0 = [ (m_x + n_x - B_x)^2 + B_y^2 - (n_x - m_x)^2 ] / (2 B_y).   (3)

Now, B is expressed in terms of M,N. Let's compute B_x and B_y.

Then B = 2Q.

But we have line MN. However, we can also compute B in terms of M and N using vector geometry: B = M + N - 2 * proj_{d}(M)?? Given the complexity, I'll attempt to carry out the algebra in a systematic way. I'll define variables:

C_x = m1 - (r/d)*(n1 - m1).  (C1)
C_y = m2 - (r/d)*(n2 - m2).  (C2)

Similarly,

D_y = n2 + (R/d)*(n2 - m2).  (D2)

Now, P = (p, 0). Condition P·C = |C|^2 / 2:

p * C_x = (C_x^2 + C_y^2) / 2.  (EqC)

Similarly, p * D_x = (D_x^2 + D_y^2) / 2. (EqD)

Since p appears linearly, we can solve for p from EqC:

p = (C_x^2 + C_y^2) / (2 C_x), provided C_x ≠ 0. Similarly, from EqD, p = (D_x^2 + D_y^2) / (2 D_x). For consistency, these must be equal. That will impose a relation that must hold because P is defined as circumcenter. So we can use either.

Now, also note that C_x could be zero. That would be a degenerate case where M and N have same x-coordinate? That's possible. But we can handle separately or argue that it's not a problem because then the line AP is vertical? Actually C_x = 0 would mean that the point C is on the y-axis. Thus, we can compute p from C or D. But maybe it's easier to compute p from the expression for P as the intersection of perpendicular bisectors, which gave linear equations. But we already have a relation. I'll proceed.

Now, also compute H. We'll need H_y. Let's compute H from the linear equations:

(1) (m1 - n1) h_x + (m2 - n2) h_y = (m1 - n1) p.   (1)

We can solve for h_y. But also note that we could compute H using formula: In triangle with vertices P,M,N, the orthocenter H can be expressed as H = P + M + N - 2 O, but we can compute O using perpendicular bisectors. However, solving the linear system is straightforward.

Let’s solve.

Similarly, (2): (n1 - p) h_x + n2 h_y = (n1 - p) m1 + n2 m2.

We can solve for h_y. It might be messy but manageable.

y0 = [ (m1 + n1 - B_x)^2 + B_y^2 - (n1 - m1)^2 ] / (2 B_y).   (3)

Alternatively, perhaps we can avoid computing y0 explicitly by using the fact that the circle (BEF) has center O, and we can compute its radius squared as |O - E|^2. The condition for tangency of horizontal line y = H_y is that (O_y - H_y)^2 = |O - E|^2. So we need to verify:

(O_y - H_y)^2 = (n1 - m1)^2 + O_y^2. That's the equation. We can compute O_y from B. But perhaps we can find a simpler expression for O_y in terms of M,N and p. Let's try to compute O_y using vector geometry.

We have E = (2 m1, 0). F = (2 n1, 0). So EF is horizontal. The perpendicular bisector of EF is vertical line x = m1 + n1. So O lies on that line. So O = (m1 + n1, y0). Now, O is also the circumcenter of BEF. So O is the intersection of perpendicular bisector of EF (vertical) and perpendicular bisector of BE. So we can compute y0 by solving the perpendicular bisector of BE. That might yield an expression that simplifies.

Let’s compute the perpendicular bisector of BE. B = (B_x, B_y), E = (2 m1, 0). The midpoint of BE is M_BE = ((B_x + 2 m1)/2, (B_y + 0)/2). The slope of BE is (0 - B_y) / (2 m1 - B_x) = -B_y / (2 m1 - B_x). So the perpendicular slope is (2 m1 - B_x) / B_y. So the equation of perpendicular bisector is:

(y - (B_y/2)) = ((2 m1 - B_x) / B_y) * (x - (B_x + 2 m1)/2).

Now, we set x = m1 + n1 (the vertical line). Then we can solve for y, which is y0. That gives:

y0 = B_y/2 + ((2 m1 - B_x) / B_y) * ( (m1 + n1) - (B_x + 2 m1)/2 ). Compute that.

Simplify:

(m1 + n1) - (B_x + 2 m1)/2 = (2(m1 + n1) - (B_x + 2 m1)) / 2 = (2m1 + 2n1 - B_x - 2m1) / 2 = (2n1 - B_x) / 2.

Thus:

y0 = B_y/2 + ((2 m1 - B_x) / B_y) * ((2n1 - B_x) / 2 = B_y/2 + ( (2 m1 - B_x)*(2n1 - B_x) ) / (2 B_y).

So:

y0 = \frac{B_y}{2} + \frac{(2 m1 - B_x)*(2n1 - B_x)}{2 B_y}.

We can combine:

y0 = \frac{B_y^2 + (2 m1 - B_x)*(2n1 - B_x)}{2 B_y}.

Now, that's an expression. But earlier we had y0 = [ (m1 + n1 - B_x)^2 + B_y^2 - (n1 - m1)^2 ] / (2 B_y). That might be equivalent after simplification. Let's check if they are the same. But I'm not sure.

Now, we also have H_y. Perhaps we can find that H_y = - (p) * something? Or maybe H_y = - (some expression) that matches y0?

Let's compute H_y from the linear equations. We have:

Equation (1): (m1 - n1) h_x + (m2 - n2) h_y = (m1 - n1) p.
Equation (2): (n1 - p) h_x + n2 h_y = (n1 - p) m1 + n2 m2.

We can solve for h_y by eliminating h_x. But perhaps we can find a more clever relationship: Since P is the circumcenter of ACD, we have that P lies on perpendicular bisector of AC and AD. That gives:

|P - A|^2 = |P - C|^2 → |P|^2 = |P - C|^2. Since A=0. So:

Let’s denote:

a1 = m1 - n1, b1 = m2 - n2. Then (1): a1 h_x + b1 h_y = a1 p.
(2): a2 = n1 - p, b2 = n2. Then (2): a2 h_x + b2 h_y = a2 m1 + b2 m2.

Solve for h_y. Use Cramer's rule.

The determinant of the system (coefficients for h_x, h_y) is:

Δ = a1 * b2 - b1 * a2 = a1 * n2 - b1 * (n1 - p).

Then:

a1 h_x + b1 h_y = a1 p.
a2 h_x + b2 h_y = a2 m1 + b2 m2.

[ a1  b1 ] [h_x] = [a1 p]
[ a2  b2 ] [h_y]   [a2 m1 + b2 m2]

Then h_y = (a1 * (a2 m1 + b2 m2) - a2 * (a1 p)) / (a1 b2 - b1 a2)? Actually compute using Cramer's rule:

Δ = a1 b2 - b1 a2.

Then:

h_y = det( [a1, a1 p; a2, a2 m1 + b2 m2] ) / Δ = (a1 * (a2 m1 + b2 m2) - a2 * (a1 p)) / Δ.

But careful: The matrix for h_y is:

[ a1, a1 p ]
[ a2, a2 m1 + b2 m2 ] 

Yes. So numerator N_y = a1 * (a2 m1 + b2 m2) - a2 * (a1 p). That's N_y = a1 a2 m1 + a1 b2 m2 - a1 a2 p.

Thus:

h_y = (a1 a2 (m1 - p) + a1 b2 m2) / Δ.

But note that a1 = m1 - n1, a2 = n1 - p, b2 = n2.

3. The line MN intersects Ω at C and Γ at D with order C-M-N-D. We can compute C and D in terms of M,N,r,R. Since M and N are centers, and the line MN is the line through M and N. Let’s denote:

a = m1 - n1, b = m2 - n2.
c = n1 - p, d = n2.

Then system:

a h_x + b h_y = a p.      (1)
c h_x + d h_y = c m1 + d m2.   (2)

We want h_y.

Compute determinant Δ = a d - b c.

Then h_y = (a * (c m1 + d m2) - c * (a p)) / Δ = (a c (m1 - p) + a d m2) / Δ.

Thus:

h_y = \frac{ a (c (m1 - p) + d m2) ) }{ Δ }.

But a = m1 - n1, c = n1 - p, d = n2. So:

h_y = \frac{ (m1 - n1) [ (n1 - p)(m1 - p) + n2 m2 ] }{ (m1 - n1) n2 - (m2 - n2)(n1 - p) }.

Now, (H - M) · (N - P) = 0. Compute N - P = (1.074 - 1.7896, 0.534 - 0) = (-0.7156, 0.534). H - M = (h_x - 0.893, h_y + 0.449). Dot:

Now, we have two equations (1) and (2). Solve.

(1): 0.181 h_x + 0.983 h_y = 0.324.
(2): 0.7156 h_x - 0.534 h_y = 0.879.

Solve. Compute determinant Δ = 0.181 * (-0.534) - 0.983 * 0.7156 = -0.0967 - 0.703 = -0.7997. Actually compute precisely:

0.181 * -0.534 = -0.096654. 0.983 * 0.7156 = 0.7029. So Δ = -0.096654 - 0.7029 = -0.799554.

Now, h_x = (0.324 * (-0.534) - 0.983 * 0.879) / Δ? Use Cramer's rule for h_x: h_x = det( [0.324, 0.983; 0.879, -0.534] ) / Δ. But careful: The system is:

a1 h_x + b1 h_y = c1.
a2 h_x + b2 h_y = c2.

Here a1=0.181, b1=0.983, c1=0.324.
a2=0.7156, b2=-0.534, c2=0.879.

So h_x = (c1 b2 - c2 b1) / (a1 b2 - a2 b1). That's (0.324 * (-0.534) - 0.879 * 0.983) / (0.181 * (-0.534) - 0.7156 * 0.983). Compute numerator: 0.324 * -0.534 = -0.1732, 0.879 * 0.983 = 0.864, so numerator = -0.1732 - 0.864 = -1.0372. Denominator: 0.181 * -0.534 = -0.0967, 0.7156 * 0.983 = 0.7029, so denominator = -0.0967 - 0.7029 = -0.7996. Then h_x = (-1.0372)/(-0.7996) = 1.295. So h_x ≈ 1.295. Then h_y from (1): 0.181*1.295 + 0.983 h_y = 0.324 → 0.234 + 0.983 h_y = 0.324 → 0.983 h_y = 0.09 → h_y ≈ 0.0916. So H ≈ (1.295, 0.0916). That's interesting. H_y is positive (0.0916). P_y = 0. So H is above x-axis? Actually H_y positive. So H is above the x-axis.

Now, compute O (circumcenter of BEF). We have M'' ≈ (0.893, -0.449), N'' ≈ (1.074, 0.534). So a = 0.893, b = -0.449; c = 1.074, d = 0.534.

Then E = (2 a, 0) = (1.786, 0). F = (2 c, 0) = (2.148, 0). So EF horizontal.

Now, compute B (reflection of A=origin across MN). First compute foot Q. MN direction d = N - M = (1.074 - 0.893, 0.534 - (-0.449)) = (0.181, 0.983). That's the same as earlier (c-a, d-b) = (0.181, 0.983). Compute M·d = (0.893 * 0.181) + (-0.449 * 0.983) = 0.1617 - 0.441 = -0.2793. Actually compute: 0.893*0.181 = 0.1617, -0.449*0.983 = -0.441, sum = -0.2793. |d|^2 = 0.181^2 + 0.983^2 = 0.0328 + 0.966 = 0.9988 ≈ 1. So t0 = - (M·d) / |d|^2 = -(-0.2793)/1 = 0.2793. Then Q = M + t0 d = (0.893, -0.449) + 0.2793*(0.181, 0.983) = (0.893 + 0.0505, -0.449 + 0.274) = (0.9435, -0.175). Then B = 2Q = (1.887, -0.35). So B ≈ (1.887, -0.35). Note that B_y is negative. O_x = a + c = 0.893 + 1.074 = 1.967. O_y = y0.

Now compute y0 from condition O is equidistant to B and E. E = (1.786, 0). B = (1.887, -0.35). O = (1.967, y0). Compute |O - B|^2 = (1.967-1.887)^2 + (y0 + 0.35)^2 = (0.08)^2 + (y0+0.35)^2 = 0.0064 + (y0+0.35)^2. |O - E|^2 = (1.967-1.786)^2 + (y0 - 0)^2 = (0.181)^2 + y0^2 = 0.0328 + y0^2.

Set equal:

0.0064 + (y0+0.35)^2 = 0.0328 + y0^2 → 0.0064 + y0^2 + 0.7 y0 + 0.1225 = 0.0328 + y0^2 → cancel y0^2 → 0.0064 + 0.7 y0 + 0.1225 = 0.0328 → 0.7 y0 + 0.1289 = 0.0328 → 0.7 y0 = -0.0961 → y0 = -0.1373. So O_y ≈ -0.1373.

C h_x + D h_y = m1 C + m2 n2.   (4a)

We want h_y. Using Cramer's rule:

Δ = A D - B C = (m1 - n1) n2 - (m2 - n2)(n1 - p).

Then h_y = (A * (m1 C + m2 n2) - C * (p A)) / Δ = (A (m1 C + m2 n2) - p A C) / Δ = A (m1 C + m2 n2 - p C) / Δ = A (C (m1 - p) + m2 n2) / Δ.

Thus:

h_y = \frac{ (m1 - n1) [ (n1 - p)(m1 - p) + m2 n2 ] }{ (m1 - n1) n2 - (m2 - n2)(n1 - p) }.   (5)

2 P·(D - A) = |D|^2 - |A|^2.  (2)

We know |C|^2 = r^2, |D|^2 = (d+R)^2, |A|^2 = r^2. So |C|^2 - |A|^2 = 0. Thus, (1) gives:

2 P·(C - A) = 0 → P·(C - A) = 0. So P·C = P·A.

Similarly, (2) gives:

2 P·(D - A) = (d+R)^2 - r^2. But we also know that A lies on Γ, so (A - N)^2 = R^2 → (x0 - d)^2 + y0^2 = R^2. And |N|^2 = d^2. So (d+R)^2 - r^2 is something like? Not obviously zero. So we have:

P·(D - A) = \frac{ (d+R)^2 - r^2 }{2}.   (2')

Now, also P·C = P·A. That's interesting.

Since C = (-r,0), P·C = -r p_x. So -r p_x = P·A. Thus, P·A = -r p_x. So A·P = -r p_x. That's a relation.

Now, we also have from the definition of P as circumcenter: |P - A|^2 = |P - C|^2. That gave earlier equation with p_y. But we can also use the relation P·A = -r p_x. That might simplify the expression for t_E and s_F.

Now, also we can find p_y from the equation |P - A|^2 = |P - C|^2. That yields:

(p_x - x0)^2 + (p_y - y0)^2 = (p_x + r)^2 + p_y^2.

Simplify: (p_x - x0)^2 + p_y^2 - 2 p_y y0 + y0^2 = (p_x + r)^2 + p_y^2 → cancel p_y^2 → (p_x - x0)^2 - 2 p_y y0 + y0^2 = (p_x + r)^2.

Thus, -2 p_y y0 = (p_x + r)^2 - (p_x - x0)^2 - y0^2.

So 2 p_y y0 = (p_x - x0)^2 + y0^2 - (p_x + r)^2.

Now, we also know that A·P = -r p_x. So x0 p_x + y0 p_y = -r p_x. That gives:

y0 p_y = -r p_x - x0 p_x = -p_x (r + x0). So p_y = -p_x (r + x0) / y0.

But we also have from the equation above an expression for p_y. That should be consistent. Let's compute p_y from that equation:

p_y = \frac{ (p_x - x0)^2 + y0^2 - (p_x + r)^2 }{ 2 y0 }.

Simplify numerator: (p_x^2 - 2 p_x x0 + x0^2) + y0^2 - (p_x^2 + 2 r p_x + r^2) = -2 p_x x0 + x0^2 + y0^2 - 2 r p_x - r^2. But x0^2 + y0^2 = r^2. So numerator = -2 p_x x0 + r^2 - 2 r p_x - r^2 = -2 p_x x0 - 2 r p_x = -2 p_x (x0 + r). So

p_y = \frac{ -2 p_x (x0 + r) }{ 2 y0 } = - \frac{ p_x (x0 + r) }{ y0 }.

That's consistent with the relation from A·P = -r p_x? Let's check: From A·P = x0 p_x + y0 p_y = -r p_x → y0 p_y = -p_x (r + x0). So p_y = -p_x (r + x0) / y0. Yes, that's exactly the same. So indeed, the relation A·P = -r p_x follows from P·C = P·A (which we derived from the circumcenter condition). So we have that.

Now, we also have from the other equation (2') that P·(D - A) = something. That might relate p_x, p_y, etc. But maybe we can find p_y directly from the expression we derived, which is p_y = -p_x (x0 + r) / y0. That's fine.

Now, also we can compute H_y = p_x (d - p_x) / p_y. So H_y = p_x (d - p_x) / ( - p_x (x0 + r) / y0 ) = - (d - p_x) y0 / (x0 + r). That's a nice simplification! Because p_x cancels. So H_y = - \frac{ (d - p_x) y0 }{ x0 + r }.

Now, compute C and D in terms of M,N. Let's denote:

We can try to compute |C|^2 in a simpler form. Since C = M - (r/d) v, we have:

|C|^2 = |M|^2 - 2 (r/d) M·v + (r^2/d^2) |v|^2 = r^2 - 2 (r/d) M·v + (r^2/d^2) d^2 = r^2 - 2 (r/d) M·v + r^2 = 2 r^2 - 2 (r/d) M·v.

Thus, |C|^2 = 2 r^2 - \frac{2 r}{d} (M·v). That's nice. Similarly, for D: D = N + (R/d) v. So |D|^2 = |N|^2 + 2 (R/d) N·v + (R^2/d^2) |v|^2 = R^2 + 2 (R/d) N·v + (R^2/d^2) d^2 = R^2 + 2 (R/d) N·v + R^2 = 2 R^2 + 2 (R/d) N·v.

So |D|^2 = 2 R^2 + \frac{2 R}{d} (N·v). Good.

Similarly, from D: p = \frac{ R^2 + \frac{R}{d} (N·v) }{ c + \frac{R}{d}(c-a) }.

I think the synthetic approach might be easier. Let's try to find a synthetic proof by analyzing angles and using properties of orthocenters.

We have:

- Ω (M), Γ (N) intersect at A,B.
- Line MN meets Ω at C (on the far side of M from N) and Γ at D (on the far side of N from M). So C-M-N-D collinear.
- P is circumcenter of ACD.
- H is orthocenter of PMN.
- AP meets Ω again at E and Γ again at F.

Goal: line through H parallel to AP is tangent to (BEF).

Observation: Since P is circumcenter of ACD, we have PA = PC = PD. Also, M is center of Ω, so MA = MC. So M lies on perpendicular bisector of AC. Similarly, N lies on perpendicular bisector of AD. So M and N are on the perpendicular bisectors of AC and AD respectively. So MP and NP are those perpendicular bisectors. Therefore, MP ⟂ AC and NP ⟂ AD. Also, AP is a line.

Now, consider the orthocenter H of triangle PMN. Since MP ⟂ AC and NP ⟂ AD. So the lines MP and NP are perpendicular to AC and AD respectively. Also, AB ⟂ MN. So we have many perpendicularities.

Maybe we can prove that AP is the Simson line of H with respect to triangle something, or that H is the Miquel point of quadrilateral formed by A, C, D, B. Let's examine quadrilateral ACDB. Also, B is the other intersection. So triangle BEF is formed by B and the second intersections of AP with circles. That's a typical configuration.

Let’s denote a_u = A·u, a_v = A·v. Then we have a_u^2 + a_v^2 = r^2, and (a_u - d)^2 + a_v^2 = R^2. Subtract: (a_u - d)^2 - a_u^2 = R^2 - r^2 → (a_u^2 - 2d a_u + d^2) - a_u^2 = R^2 - r^2 → -2d a_u + d^2 = R^2 - r^2 → 2d a_u = d^2 + r^2 - R^2 → a_u = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2) / (2d). So that's x0. And a_v^2 = r^2 - a_u^2. Since a_v could be positive or negative. We choose A such that a_v > 0 (above MN). Then B has a_v = -a_v.

Thus A = a_u * u + a_v * v.

Now P = p_u * u + p_v * v, with p_u = (d + R - r)/2. And we have relation from PA = PC: |P - A|^2 = |P - C|^2. C = -r * u. So (P - C) = (p_u + r) * u + p_v * v. So PC^2 = (p_u + r)^2 + p_v^2.

Similarly, PA^2 = (p_u - a_u)^2 + (p_v - a_v)^2.

Set equal:

(p_u - a_u)^2 + (p_v - a_v)^2 = (p_u + r)^2 + p_v^2.

Expand:

(p_u^2 - 2 p_u a_u + a_u^2) + (p_v^2 - 2 p_v a_v + a_v^2) = (p_u^2 + 2 r p_u + r^2) + p_v^2.

Cancel p_u^2 and p_v^2:

-2 p_u a_u + a_u^2 - 2 p_v a_v + a_v^2 = 2 r p_u + r^2.

But note a_u^2 + a_v^2 = r^2. So a_u^2 + a_v^2 - r^2 = 0. Then we have:

-2 p_u a_u - 2 p_v a_v = 2 r p_u.

Thus:

-2 p_u a_u - 2 p_v a_v = 2 r p_u → divide by 2: -p_u a_u - p_v a_v = r p_u.

So p_v a_v = -p_u a_u - r p_u = -p_u (a_u + r). Hence:

p_v = -p_u (a_u + r) / a_v.

That's consistent with earlier (y_P). So we have p_v expressed.

Now we can compute a_v^2 = r^2 - a_u^2. So a_v = sqrt(r^2 - a_u^2). That's okay.

So M and P are on the same line (perpendicular bisector of AC). Similarly, N and P are on the same line (perpendicular bisector of AD). So P is the intersection of lines that are the perpendicular bisectors of AC and AD. Since M is on perpendicular bisector of AC, and N is on perpendicular bisector of AD, but P is the unique point common to both perpendicular bisectors. So P is the point where these two lines intersect. That gives a relationship: P is the radical center? Not sure.

Now from our numeric, we observed that P lies on Ω (distance from M = r). Let's check if that is generally true. Compute MP^2 = |P|^2. Since M is origin. We have P = (p_u, p_v). We know that PA^2 = PC^2. Also, A and C are on Ω. So we have MA = r, MC = r. The perpendicular bisector of AC passes through M. So M is on that line. The circumcenter P is also on that line. So P lies on the line through M that is the perpendicular bisector of AC. That line is not necessarily the line MN. So P is somewhere on that line. But is it forced that MP = r? That would mean P is on Ω. Let's test with another numeric. Choose r=1, R=1.2, d=1. Then x0 = (1 + 1 - 1.44)/2 = (2 - 1.44)/2 = 0.56/2 = 0.28. y0 = sqrt(1 - 0.0784) = sqrt(0.9216) = 0.96. Then p_u = (1 + 1.2 - 1)/2 = (1.2)/2 = 0.6. p_v = -p_u (x0 + r)/y0 = -0.6 * (0.28+1)/0.96 = -0.6 * 1.28 / 0.96 = -0.768 / 0.96 = -0.8. So P = (0.6, -0.8). Then MP^2 = 0.36 + 0.64 = 1. So again P lies on Ω. That's interesting. Let's test with r=2, R=3, d=1 (MN=1). But then r < R, and intersection condition: R - r = 1, d = 1 => circles tangent internally, so not two distinct points. So we need d > R - r. With d=1, we need R - r < 1. So R < r+1. So the radii difference is less than 1. So r and R are close. In our test, r=1, R=1.2, difference 0.2 < 1. So MP^2 = 1. So it seems that P lies on Ω. Let's test with r=1, R=1.5, difference 0.5, we got MP^2 ≈ 1. So indeed P is on Ω. That suggests a general fact: For two intersecting circles with centers M, N, if we take the circumcenter P of triangle ACD (where C is the point on Ω on line MN opposite N, D is the point on Γ on line MN opposite M), then P lies on Ω. Is that always true? Let's try to prove.

We have M = 0, N = (d,0). Then C = -r u, D = (d+R) u. A is intersection. The perpendicular bisector of AC passes through M. The perpendicular bisector of AD passes through N. But P is the intersection of those two lines. The line through M that is the perpendicular bisector of AC is the line consisting of points X such that XA = XC, which is the perpendicular bisector of AC. Since M lies on that line, the line is determined. Similarly, the line through N that is the perpendicular bisector of AD is determined. P is the unique point on both lines. So P is the intersection of the perpendicular bisector of AC and the perpendicular bisector of AD. But is it guaranteed that MP = r? That would be equivalent to P lying on Ω. Let's compute MP^2 using coordinates.

We have P = (p_u, p_v). We derived A·P = -p_u r. That's a relation. Also, we have |P - A|^2 = (p_u + r)^2 + p_v^2. But maybe we can compute |P|^2 = |P|^2. Since A is on Ω, |A|^2 = r^2. And we know that |P - A|^2 = |P - C|^2. That gave p_v = -p_u (x0 + r)/y0. That seems to imply that P is not necessarily on Ω. But numeric suggests it is. Let's compute MP^2 from coordinates with variables, using the expression for p_v.

We have:

M = (0,0). P = (p_u, p_v). So MP^2 = p_u^2 + p_v^2.

We can compute p_v^2 = p_u^2 (x0 + r)^2 / y0^2. And we also have y0^2 = r^2 - x0^2.

So MP^2 = p_u^2 + p_u^2 (x0 + r)^2 / (r^2 - x0^2) = p_u^2 * [1 + (x0 + r)^2 / (r^2 - x0^2)].

Now p_u = (d + R - r)/2. But we set d=1. So p_u = (1 + R - r)/2. Also x0 = (1 + r^2 - R^2)/2.

Let's compute MP^2 in terms of r and R. Simplify the bracket:

B = 1 + (x0 + r)^2 / (r^2 - x0^2). Note that r^2 - x0^2 = (r - x0)(r + x0). Also (x0 + r)^2 = (r + x0)^2. So:

B = 1 + (r + x0)^2 / ((r - x0)(r + x0)) = 1 + (r + x0) / (r - x0). Because (r + x0)^2 / ((r - x0)(r + x0)) = (r + x0) / (r - x0). So:

MP^2 = p_u^2 * [1 + (r + x0) / (r - x0)] = p_u^2 * [(r - x0) + (r + x0)] / (r - x0) = p_u^2 * (2r) / (r - x0).

Thus MP^2 = p_u^2 * (2r) / (r - x0). Now we also have relation between x0, r, R. Since x0 = (1 + r^2 - R^2)/2, then r - x0 = r - (1 + r^2 - R^2)/2 = (2r - 1 - r^2 + R^2)/2 = (R^2 - r^2 + 2r - 1)/2. That seems not obviously something like (something). But maybe we can compute p_u^2 * (2r) / (r - x0) and simplify to r^2 (i.e., MP^2 = r^2). Let's try.

We have p_u = (1 + R - r)/2. Then p_u^2 = ( (1 + R - r)^2 ) / 4.

Now compute r - x0. x0 = (1 + r^2 - R^2)/2. So:

r - x0 = r - (1 + r^2 - R^2)/2 = (2r - 1 - r^2 + R^2) / 2.

Now MP^2 = p_u^2 * (2r) / (r - x0) = ((1 + R - r)^2 / 4) * (2r) / ((2r - 1 - r^2 + R^2)/2) = ((1 + R - r)^2 / 4) * (2r) * (2 / (2r - 1 - r^2 + R^2)) = ((1 + R - r)^2 / 4) * (4r) / (2r - 1 - r^2 + R^2) = ((1 + R - r)^2 * r) / (2r - 1 - r^2 + R^2).

So MP^2 = r * (1 + R - r)^2 / (2r - 1 - r^2 + R^2). That's not obviously r^2. Let's test with numeric: r=1, R=1.2 → 1 + R - r = 1.2, square = 1.44, numerator = 1.44. denominator: 2r - 1 - r^2 + R^2 = 2 - 1 - 1 + 1.44 = 1.44. So MP^2 = 1.44 / 1.44 = 1. So indeed MP^2 = 1 = r^2. So in that numeric, it works. Let's test with r=1, R=1.5 → 1+R-r = 1.5, square=2.25. denominator: 2 - 1 - 1 + 2.25 = 2.25. So MP^2 = 2.25 / 2.25 = 1. So it seems that (1+R - r)^2 = (2r - 1 - r^2 + R^2) ??? Let's check identity:

We have r=1, R=1.2 → 1+R - r = 1.2, square = 1.44. Denominator: 2r - 1 - r^2 + R^2 = 2 - 1 - 1 + 1.44 = 1.44. So indeed they are equal. That suggests that (1+R - r)^2 = 2r - 1 - r^2 + R^2. But is this identity true for all r,R? Let's expand both sides.

Left: (1 + R - r)^2 = (1 + (R - r))^2 = 1 + 2(R - r) + (R - r)^2 = 1 + 2R - 2r + R^2 - 2Rr + r^2.
Right: 2r - 1 - r^2 + R^2.

Set them equal: 1 + 2R - 2r + R^2 - 2Rr + r^2 = 2r - 1 - r^2 + R^2. Cancel R^2. Then:

1 + 2R - 2r - 2Rr + r^2 = 2r - 1 - r^2.
Bring terms: 1 + 2R - 2r - 2Rr + r^2 - 2r + 1 + r^2 = 0? Actually let's move all to one side:

(1 + 2R - 2r - 2Rr + r^2) - (2r - 1 - r^2) = 0 → 1 + 2R - 2r - 2Rr + r^2 - 2r + 1 + r^2 = 0? Wait careful: subtract: (1 + 2R - 2r - 2Rr + r^2) - (2r - 1 - r^2) = 1 + 2R - 2r - 2Rr + r^2 - 2r + 1 + r^2? Actually -(2r) = -2r, -(-1) = +1, -(-r^2) = +r^2. So it becomes: 1 + 2R - 2r - 2Rr + r^2 - 2r + 1 + r^2 = 2 + 2R - 4r - 2Rr + 2r^2. So we need this to be zero. That seems unlikely to be identically zero. So the equality is not an identity. But in our numeric examples, it happened because we set d=1, and also used the intersection condition which relates r and R. Let's examine: We have MN = d = 1. The condition for intersecting circles is |R - r| < 1 < R + r. That's a region. For given r and R satisfying that, is it always true that MP^2 = r^2? Let's compute MP^2 more generally with d as a variable. We have:

M = 0, N = d u. Then:

x0 = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2) / (2d). Then p_u = (d + R - r) / 2. Then we derived MP^2 = p_u^2 * (2r) / (r - x0) (after simplifying). Let's derive that expression generally.

We have:

MP^2 = p_u^2 + p_v^2. But we also expressed p_v^2 = p_u^2 (x0 + r)^2 / y0^2. And y0^2 = r^2 - x0^2. Then MP^2 = p_u^2 [1 + (x0 + r)^2 / (r^2 - x0^2)] = p_u^2 [ (r^2 - x0^2) + (x0 + r)^2 ] / (r^2 - x0^2). Compute numerator: (r^2 - x0^2) + (x0^2 + 2r x0 + r^2) = 2r^2 + 2r x0. So MP^2 = p_u^2 * (2r^2 + 2r x0) / (r^2 - x0^2). But earlier we simplified to p_u^2 * (2r) / (r - x0). Let's check.

We have r^2 - x0^2 = (r - x0)(r + x0). Then:

MP^2 = p_u^2 * (2r^2 + 2r x0) / ((r - x0)(r + x0)) = p_u^2 * (2r(r + x0)) / ((r - x0)(r + x0)) = p_u^2 * (2r) / (r - x0). So that's correct.

Thus MP^2 = p_u^2 * (2r) / (r - x0). Now x0 is expressed in terms of d, r, R. Let's compute r - x0.

x0 = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2) / (2d). Then:

r - x0 = r - (d^2 + r^2 - R^2) / (2d) = (2dr - d^2 - r^2 + R^2) / (2d) = (R^2 - (d^2 + r^2 - 2dr)) / (2d) = (R^2 - (d - r)^2) / (2d). Since (d^2 + r^2 - 2dr) = (d - r)^2.

Thus:

r - x0 = (R^2 - (d - r)^2) / (2d).

Similarly, p_u = (d + R - r) / 2. So:

MP^2 = p_u^2 * (2r) / (r - x0) = ((d + R - r)^2 / 4) * (2r) / ( (R^2 - (d - r)^2) / (2d) ) = ((d + R - r)^2 / 4) * (2r) * (2d / (R^2 - (d - r)^2)) = ((d + R - r)^2 / 4) * (4r d) / (R^2 - (d - r)^2) = (d + R - r)^2 * r d / (R^2 - (d - r)^2).

So MP^2 = r d (d + R - r)^2 / (R^2 - (d - r)^2).

Now note that R^2 - (d - r)^2 = (R - (d - r)) (R + (d - r)). But also (d + R - r)^2 appears. Maybe we can relate these using the intersection condition. The circles intersect, so we have |R - r| < d < R + r. Since r < R, we have R - r < d. Also d < R + r. So (d - r) is positive? d > r? Not necessarily. d could be less than r. For example, if r=1, R=1.2, d=1, then d > r? Actually 1 > 1? No, d=1 equals r=1, but then R - r = 0.2 < d = 1, so d > R - r. But is d > r? 1 > 1? No, it's equal. So d = r is allowed? Then R^2 - (d - r)^2 = R^2 > 0. And (d + R - r) = (r + R - r) = R. So MP^2 = r d * R^2 / R^2 = r d = r^2. So MP^2 = r^2, so P lies on Ω. That matches numeric.

If d > r, then (d - r) > 0, and we can compute MP^2. Is it always r^2? Let's see.

We have MP^2 = r d (d + R - r)^2 / (R^2 - (d - r)^2). We want to see if this simplifies to r^2 given the relation between d, r, R from intersection (maybe also from the fact that A is on both circles, which we already used). But we haven't used any further relation beyond the existence of intersection. So we can try to express (d + R - r)^2 / (R^2 - (d - r)^2) in terms of d, r, R. Let's simplify that fraction.

Let’s denote:

A = (d + R - r)^2, B = (R^2 - (d - r)^2). Compute B:

B = R^2 - (d - r)^2 = (R - (d - r)) (R + (d - r)) = (R - d + r) (R + d - r) = (R + r - d) (R + d - r). That's interesting.

Now A = (d + R - r)^2. So MP^2 = r d * A / B.

Now note that A / B is something like ( (R + d - r)^2 ) / ( (R + r - d)(R + d - r) ) = (R + d - r) / (R + r - d). So:

MP^2 = r d * (R + d - r) / (R + r - d). That's a simpler expression. Let's verify:

We have B = (R + r - d)(R + d - r). Indeed:

R^2 - (d - r)^2 = (R - (d - r))(R + (d - r)) = (R - d + r)(R + d - r) = (R + r - d)(R + d - r). So B = (R + r - d)(R + d - r). Then:

A / B = (d + R - r)^2 / ((R + r - d)(R + d - r)). But note that (d + R - r) = (R + d - r). So A = (R + d - r)^2. Then:

A / B = (R + d - r)^2 / ((R + r - d)(R + d - r)) = (R + d - r) / (R + r - d).

Thus:

MP^2 = r d * (R + d - r) / (R + r - d).

So MP^2 = r d * (R + d - r) / (R + r - d).

Now is this equal to r^2? That would require:

r d * (R + d - r) / (R + r - d) = r^2 → d * (R + d - r) / (R + r - d) = r → d (R + d - r) = r (R + r - d).

But we can check if this holds under the intersection condition. Let's examine.

We have d, r, R satisfying |R - r| < d < R + r. So d > R - r, and d < R + r. Not necessarily equality. The expression for MP^2 is derived without any additional assumptions. So my earlier numeric with r=1, d=1 gave MP^2=1, but that's because r=1 and d=1, and the expression MP^2 = r d * (R + d - r) / (R + r - d) simplifies to r^2 if d = r? The problem only says "Let Ω and Γ be circles with centres M and N, respectively, such that the radius of Ω is less than the radius of Γ. Suppose Ω and Γ intersect at two distinct points A and B. Line MN intersects Ω at C and Γ at D, so that C, M, N, D lie on MN in that order." That's all. It does not specify any relationship between MN and radii. So d can be any value satisfying the intersection conditions. So d may be less than r, equal to r, or greater than r. But note the order C, M, N, D. Now, we need to prove the tangency. So we need to find a way to prove the statement without heavy algebra. Now we need to prove that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to (BEF). So we can compute coordinates in terms of d and R. That seems doable. But we must be careful about square roots. However, many simplifications occur due to A·P = -p_u r (r=1). And also we can compute p_v^2 and other things. Let's proceed with r=1.

Set r = 1. Then M = (0,0), N = (d,0) with d > 0. C = (-1,0). D = (d + R,0). A is intersection. Then:

x0 = (d^2 + 1 - R^2) / (2d). y0 = sqrt(1 - x0^2). Then A = (x0, y0). B = (x0, -y0).

p_u = (d + R - 1) / 2.
p_v = -p_u (x0 + 1) / y0.

Now H = (p_u, p_u (d - p_u) / p_v). Since M = 0, N = (d,0). That's correct.

Now we need E and F. Compute:

v = P - A. t_E = 2 r (p_u + r) / |P - A|^2 = 2 * 1 * (p_u + 1) / |P - A|^2. Because r=1. So t_E = 2 (p_u + 1) / |P - A|^2.

Similarly, s_F = -2 (A - N)·v / |v|^2. But we can also compute (A - N)·v in terms of known quantities. Let's compute A - N = (x0 - d, y0). Then (A - N)·v = (x0 - d)*(p_u - x0) + y0*(p_v - y0). That's messy. But perhaps we can find a simpler expression for s_F using the fact that F is the second intersection of line AP with Γ. Since we have A·P = -p_u r = -p_u (since r=1). And also N·P = p_u. Maybe we can compute (A - N)·P and (A - N)·A. Let's try.

(A - N)·v = (A - N)·(P - A) = (A - N)·P - (A - N)·A = (A·P - N·P) - (|A|^2 - N·A) = A·P - N·P - r^2 + N·A.

Now A·P = -p_u, N·P = p_u. So A·P - N·P = -p_u - p_u = -2p_u.

Then (A - N)·v = -2p_u - r^2 + N·A = -2p_u - 1 + x0. (since r=1, r^2=1). So:

(A - N)·v = x0 - 2p_u - 1.

Now we also have expression for x0 in terms of d and R. x0 = (d^2 + 1 - R^2) / (2d). p_u = (d + R - 1) / 2. So compute x0 - 2p_u - 1.

2p_u = d + R - 1. So:

x0 - 2p_u - 1 = x0 - (d + R - 1) - 1 = x0 - d - R + 1 - 1 = x0 - d - R.

Thus (A - N)·v = x0 - d - R.

Now x0 - d - R = (d^2 + 1 - R^2) / (2d) - d - R = (d^2 + 1 - R^2 - 2d^2 - 2dR) / (2d) = ( -d^2 + 1 - R^2 - 2dR ) / (2d). That's not obviously simplifying.

But we can also compute (A - N)·v using other relations. |M| = r, |N| = R. Also, E is on Ω and on AP (x-axis). So E = (e,0) satisfies |E - M| = r. Similarly, F = (f,0) satisfies |F - N| = R.

Now, M and N are points with distances from origin equal to r and R respectively. So we can param:

M = (r cos α, r sin α) for some α.
N = (R cos β, R sin β) for some β.

Now, the circles intersect at A (origin) and B. The line AB is the common chord. Since A is origin, AB is a line through origin. The common chord is perpendicular to MN. So MN ⟂ AB. That gives a relation between the angles. Let's compute MN vector = N - M. The vector AB is the line through origin and B. Since A is origin, B is a point on both circles, so B is some point. The common chord is AB. So AB is perpendicular to MN. So (N - M) · (B) = 0? Actually AB is the line through origin and B, so direction is B. So B is a vector. So (N - M) · B = 0. That's one relation.

Also, B is the second intersection, so it satisfies |B - M| = r and |B - N| = R. Since A is origin, B is some point.

We can also derive that B is the reflection of A across the line MN? No, AB is perpendicular to MN, but the midpoint of AB lies on MN. So the line MN is the perpendicular bisector of AB. That's a known fact: For two intersecting circles, the line connecting the centers is the perpendicular bisector of the common chord. So MN ⟂ AB, and MN passes through the midpoint of AB. So if A is origin, then the midpoint of AB is some point on MN. So AB is perpendicular to MN, and the midpoint lies on MN. So we can use that.

Now, we have A = origin, so B is some vector. Then the midpoint M_AB = B/2 lies on MN. So (B/2) is on line MN. So B is collinear with M and N? Not exactly, but B/2 is on MN. So the line MN passes through B/2. So B/2 is a point on MN. That's a relation.

Also, MN is the line through M and N. So B/2 lies on MN. So B is on the line that is the homothety of MN? So C is one of the two points. We need to determine which one is C based on the order C, M, N, D. The order along MN is C, M, N, D. So M is between C and N. So C is on the opposite side of M from N. That means C is the point on MN such that M is between C and N. So C is on the ray opposite to N from M. So we can param: Let M be the center. Then points on line MN are M + t * (N - M). For t=0 → M. For t=1 → N. Then C is at some t < 0 (since C is on the opposite side of M from N). So C = M + t_C * (N - M) with t_C < 0. Also, the distance from M to C is r. So |t_C| * |N - M| = r. So |t_C| = r / d, where d = MN. Since t_C is negative, t_C = -r / d. So C = M - (r / d) * (N - M) = M - (r / d) * (N - M). Similarly, D is on Γ such that N is between M and D, so D is on the ray beyond N from M, with t_D > 1. D = N + (R / d) * (N - M) (since ND = R). So D = N + (R / d) * (N - M). That's consistent.

So we can compute C and D in terms of M,N,r,R.

Now, P is circumcenter of ACD. Since A is origin, we can compute P as the intersection of perpendicular bisectors. Perhaps we can find an expression for P using vector formulas. For triangle with vertices A=0, C, D. The circumcenter is the point P such that |P|^2 = |P - C|^2 = |P - D|^2. So:

|P|^2 = |P - C|^2 → |P|^2 = |P|^2 - 2 P·C + |C|^2 → 2 P·C = |C|^2. Similarly, 2 P·D = |D|^2. So P satisfies:

P·C = |C|^2 / 2, P·D = |D|^2 / 2.

Thus P is the solution to these linear equations. Since C and D are not collinear (they are not parallel generally), there is a unique P. This is a nice expression. Define:

Observations:

1. Since P is circumcenter of ACD, we have PA = PC = PD. So P is equidistant to A, C, D. So P lies on perpendicular bisector of AC and AD.
2. M is center of Ω, so MA = MC = r. So M lies on perpendicular bisector of AC. Thus M and P are on the same line (perpendicular bisector of AC). Similarly, N and P are on the same line (perpendicular bisector of AD). So lines MP and NP are the perpendicular bisectors of AC and AD respectively. Therefore, MP ⟂ AC and NP ⟂ AD.
3. Also, AB ⟂ MN. So AB is perpendicular to MN.

Now, H is orthocenter of triangle PMN. So H is intersection of altitudes: from P (perpendicular to MN), from M (perpendicular to PN), from N (perpendicular to PM). So we have:

- PH ⟂ MN.
- MH ⟂ PN.
- NH ⟂ PM.

We also know that MP ⟂ AC. So MP is perpendicular to AC. Similarly, NP ⟂ AD. So we have perpendicularities.

Compute with r=1,d=1,R=1.2. Then:

x0 = (1 + 1 - 1.44)/2 = (2 - 1.44)/2 = 0.56/2 = 0.28. y0 = sqrt(1 - 0.0784) = sqrt(0.9216) = 0.96. So A = (0.28, 0.96). B = (0.28, -0.96). C = (-1,0). D = (1 + 1.2, 0) = (2.2,0). Then p_u = (1 + 1.2 - 1)/2 = (1.2)/2 = 0.6. p_v = -0.6 * (x0 + r) / y0 = -0.6 * (0.28+1) / 0.96 = -0.6 * 1.28 / 0.96 = -0.768 / 0.96 = -0.8. So P = (0.6, -0.8). Then H_v = p_u (d - p_u) / p_v = 0.6 * (1 - 0.6) / (-0.8) = 0.6 * 0.4 / -0.8 = 0.24 / -0.8 = -0.3. So H = (0.6, -0.3). AP direction: P - A = (0.6 - 0.28, -0.8 - 0.96) = (0.32, -1.76). So slope = -1.76/0.32 = -5.5. So line L (through H parallel to AP) has param (0.6, -0.3) + s*(0.32, -1.76). Now compute E: t_E = 2 r (p_u + r) / |P - A|^2. r=1, p_u=0.6 => p_u+r=1.6. |P-A|^2 = (0.32)^2 + (-1.76)^2 = 0.1024 + 3.0976 = 3.2. So t_E = 2*1.6 / 3.2 = 3.2 / 3.2 = 1. So E = A + 1 * (P-A) = P. So E = P. That's interesting! So in this numeric, E coincides with P. That suggests that P lies on Ω. Indeed, we observed earlier that with d=1, r=1, R=1.2, P is on Ω (distance from M is 1). That's consistent. So in this case, E = P. Similarly, F: s_F = -2 (A - N)·v / |v|^2. N = (1,0). A - N = (0.28 - 1, 0.96) = (-0.72, 0.96). v = P - A = (0.32, -1.76). Dot = (-0.72)*0.32 + 0.96*(-1.76) = -0.2304 - 1.6896 = -1.92. So (A-N)·v = -1.92. Then s_F = -2 * (-1.92) / 3.2 = 3.84 / 3.2 = 1.2. Then F = A + 1.2 v = (0.28,0.96) + 1.2*(0.32,-1.76) = (0.28+0.384, 0.96 - 2.112) = (0.664, -1.152). So F is some point.

Let's compute the midpoint of BE: M_BE = ((0.28+0.6)/2, (-0.96-0.8)/2) = (0.44, -0.88). Slope of BE = (0.16)/(0.32) = 0.5. Perpendicular slope = -2. So line: (y + 0.88) = -2 (x - 0.44). That's one line. Midpoint of BF: M_BF = ((0.28+0.664)/2, (-0.96-1.152)/2) = (0.472, -1.056). Slope of BF = (-0.192)/(0.384) = -0.5. Perpendicular slope = 2. So line: (y + 1.056) = 2 (x - 0.472). Second: y = -1.056 + 2(x - 0.472) = -1.056 + 2x - 0.944 = 2x - 2.0. So y = 2x - 2.0.
Set equal: -2x = 2x - 2 → 4x = 2 → x = 0.5, then y = -1. So O = (0.5, -1). Radius squared = distance^2 to B: (0.28-0.5)^2 + (-0.96+1)^2 = (-0.22)^2 + (0.04)^2 = 0.0484 + 0.0016 = 0.05. So radius = sqrt(0.05) ≈ 0.2236. Now, line L passes through H (0.6,-0.3) with direction v = (0.32, -1.76). Its slope is -5.5. The distance from O (0.5,-1) to line L: We can compute the line equation. Let's compute the line L param. Use vector n perpendicular to v. For v = (0.32, -1.76). A perpendicular vector is (1.76, 0.32) (swap and change sign). Dot v·(1.76,0.32) = 0.32*1.76 + (-1.76)*0.32 = 0.5632 - 0.5632 = 0. So n = (1.76, 0.32). So line L: points X such that (X - H)·n = 0. Compute H = (0.6,-0.3). Then n = (1.76, 0.32). Distance from O to L = |(O - H)·n| / |n|. O - H = (0.5-0.6, -1+0.3) = (-0.1, -0.7). Dot = (-0.1)*1.76 + (-0.7)*0.32 = -0.176 - 0.224 = -0.4. |n| = sqrt(1.76^2 + 0.32^2) = sqrt(3.0976 + 0.1024) = sqrt(3.2) ≈ 1.78885. So distance = 0.4 / 1.78885 ≈ 0.2236, which equals radius. So indeed L is tangent. So the numeric test confirms the statement.

So O = (m_x + n_x, y0) for some y0. The radius squared is |O - E|^2 = (m_x + n_x - 2 m_x)^2 + (y0 - 0)^2 = (n_x - m_x)^2 + y0^2. Similarly, |O - F|^2 = (m_x + n_x - 2 n_x)^2 + y0^2 = (m_x - n_x)^2 + y0^2. So they are equal. So indeed, O_x = m_x + n_x. So the center's x-coordinate is sum of the x-coordinates of M and N.

Now, also B is some point (x_B, y_B). Since A=0 is an intersection, B is on both circles, so |B - M| = r and |B - N| = R. Also, MN is perpendicular bisector of AB. So MN ⟂ AB, and the midpoint of AB lies on MN. That gives relations.

Now, P is (p,0). Since P is circumcenter of ACD, we have P·C = |C|^2/2 and P·D = |D|^2/2. We can also find expressions for C and D in terms of M,N. That will relate p to M,N,r,R.

Now, H is orthocenter of triangle PMN. Since we know P,M,N coordinates, we can compute H. Perhaps we can compute H_y in terms of m_x, n_x, m_y, n_y, p. And then show that |y0 - H_y| = radius. But we also need to determine y0 (the y-coordinate of the center O). That depends on B and E,F. Since O is the circumcenter of BEF, we need to compute O's y-coordinate. That might be expressed in terms of B and the known points.

Maybe we can find that the circle (BEF) has center O = (m_x + n_x, - (something) ). Actually from the numeric with A=0, we need to compute. But perhaps there is a known relationship: In this configuration, the circle (BEF) is actually the circle with diameter the segment connecting the reflections of P across M and N? Not.

Let's try to compute O using the formula for circumcenter in terms of coordinates. Since we have E and F on x-axis, we can compute O as intersection of perpendicular bisector of EF (vertical line x = m_x + n_x) and perpendicular bisector of BE (or BF). So O = (m_x + n_x, y0). Then y0 can be found from the condition that O is equidistant to B and E: |O - B|^2 = |O - E|^2. That gives an equation. So we can solve for y0 in terms of B and M,N. That's manageable. Then we also have H_y. So we need to show that |y0 - H_y| = |O - E|. That is a single equation. That seems plausible.

Now, we also have P is (p,0). And we can compute H_y from P,M,N. There is a formula for orthocenter in coordinates: For triangle with vertices A(x1,y1), B(x2,y2), C(x3,y3), the orthocenter H can be expressed as:

H = (x1 + x2 + x3 - 2 * O_x, y1 + y2 + y3 - 2 * O_y) where O is circumcenter. But we can compute H directly using the fact that H is the unique point such that (H - A) is perpendicular to (B - C), etc. There is also a formula using vector cross product: H = A + B + C - 2 * (circumcenter). But we can also compute H by solving:

(H - P) · (M - N) = 0.
(H - M) · (N - P) = 0.
(H - N) · (M - P) = 0.

That's three equations, but two are independent. We can solve. Since we are only interested in H_y, maybe we can express H_y in terms of P,M,N.

Let’s denote:

P = (p,0). M = (m_x, m_y). N = (n_x, n_y). Then compute H = (h_x, h_y). From (H - P) · (M - N) = 0:

(h_x - p, h_y) · (m_x - n_x, m_y - n_y) = 0 → (h_x - p)*(m_x - n_x) + h_y*(m_y - n_y) = 0.   (A)

From (H - M) · (N - P) = 0:

(h_x - m_x, h_y - m_y) · (n_x - p, n_y - 0) = 0 → (h_x - m_x)*(n_x - p) + (h_y - m_y)*n_y = 0.   (B)

Similarly, (H - N) · (M - P) = 0 gives another equation. But we can solve.

We can also use the fact that H is the orthocenter, so H = P + M + N - 2 O, but O can be computed using perpendicular bisectors. But maybe we can find a simpler expression: Since P is on x-axis, we might consider the altitudes. The altitude from P is perpendicular to MN. So H lies on the line through P perpendicular to MN. That line is not necessarily vertical. But we have H also lies on altitude from M, which is perpendicular to PN. So that's messy.

But perhaps we can use vector algebra to derive H_y in terms of m_x, n_x, m_y, n_y, p. Let's attempt.

We have unknowns h_x, h_y. Equations:

(1) (h_x - p)*(m_x - n_x) + h_y*(m_y - n_y) = 0.
(2) (h_x - m_x)*(n_x - p) + (h_y - m_y)*n_y = 0.

We can solve for h_x and h_y. However, these are linear equations? They involve products like (h_x - p)*(m_x - n_x). That's linear in h_x, h_y. So they are linear equations. Indeed, (1) is: (m_x - n_x) h_x + (m_y - n_y) h_y = (m_x - n_x) p. That's linear. (2) expands: (n_x - p) h_x + n_y h_y = (n_x - p) m_x + n_y m_y. That's also linear. So we can solve.

Thus, H is determined by solving two linear equations. So we can compute h_x and h_y.

Now, also we have E = (2 m_x, 0), F = (2 n_x, 0). So we can compute O = (m_x + n_x, y0) where y0 is solved from |O - B|^2 = |O - E|^2. But B is unknown. However, we can express B in terms of M,N,r,R. Since A=0 is an intersection, we can solve for B. Let's do that.

We have:

|M| = r, |N| = R. Also, B lies on both circles, so |B - M| = r, |B - N| = R. Since A=0 also satisfies those. So B is the second intersection. The line MN is the perpendicular bisector of AB. So B is the reflection of A across the line MN? No, reflecting across a line gives a point such that the line is the perpendicular bisector of the segment connecting the point and its reflection. Indeed, if you reflect a point across a line, the line is the perpendicular bisector of the segment connecting the point and its image. So if MN is the perpendicular bisector of AB, then B is the reflection of A across the line MN. Since A is origin, B is the reflection of the origin across the line MN. That is a nice characterization. So B is the point obtained by reflecting the origin across the line MN. That's a linear transformation (reflection). So we can compute B as:

B = 2 * projection of origin onto line MN? Actually reflecting a point across a line: If line L is given by a point X0 and a direction vector v, then the reflection of point P across L is: P' = P + 2 * (proj_{n}(P - X0))? Let's derive. For a line through the origin, reflection is simply 2 * projection onto the line? Actually reflection across a line through the origin: if line L passes through origin with unit direction u, then reflection of a vector x is 2*(x·u) u - x. That's the reflection across L. But here MN is a line not necessarily through the origin. So we need to reflect the origin across that line. That's a bit different. The reflection of the origin across a line L (not through origin) is twice the foot of perpendicular from origin to L. Indeed, if L is a line, then the reflection of a point P across L is P' = P + 2 * (proj_{n}(P - X0))? Actually we can compute: Let F be the foot of perpendicular from origin to L. Thus, B = 2 * foot, where foot is the orthogonal projection of origin onto line MN. So B is determined by M and N.

Now, we also have that M and N are on MN. So we can compute the foot. So we can compute F.

Now, H is orthocenter of PMN. So we need to relate H_y to the geometry. Perhaps we can find an expression for H_y in terms of M,N, and p. Let's attempt to compute H_y from the linear equations.

Equation (1): (m_x - n_x) h_x + (m_y - n_y) h_y = (m_x - n_x) p.   (1)

Equation (2): (n_x - p) h_x + n_y h_y = (n_x - p) m_x + n_y m_y.   (2)

We can solve for h_y.

Similarly, we can find y0 (the y-coordinate of the center O of circle BEF). Since O = (m_x + n_x, y0). The condition that O is equidistant to B and E gives:

|O - B|^2 = |O - E|^2. So:

(m_x + n_x - B_x)^2 + (y0 - B_y)^2 = (m_x + n_x - 2 m_x)^2 + (y0 - 0)^2 = (n_x - m_x)^2 + y0^2.

Thus:

(m_x + n_x - B_x)^2 + (y0 - B_y)^2 = (n_x - m_x)^2 + y0^2.

Expand:

(m_x + n_x - B_x)^2 + y0^2 - 2 y0 B_y + B_y^2 = (n_x - m_x)^2 + y0^2.

Cancel y0^2:

(m_x + n_x - B_x)^2 - 2 y0 B_y + B_y^2 = (n_x - m_x)^2.

Thus:

- 2 y0 B_y = (n_x - m_x)^2 - (m_x + n_x - B_x)^2 - B_y^2.

So we can solve for y0:

y0 = [ (m_x + n_x - B_x)^2 + B_y^2 - (n_x - m_x)^2 ] / (2 B_y), provided B_y ≠ 0. B_y is the y-coordinate of B. Since B is the second intersection and not on MN generally, B_y is non-zero (unless circles are tangent). So that's okay.

F = M + t0 d, where t0 satisfies (M + t0 d) · d = 0? Actually we want the foot from origin, so the vector from foot to origin (i.e., -F) is perpendicular to L. So F is such that F ⟂ d (since F is the point on L, and the line from F to origin is perpendicular to L). So (F) · d = 0? Because F is the vector from origin to foot, and it should be perpendicular to direction d. Indeed, if we have a line L with direction d, then the foot of perpendicular from origin to L is the point F on L such that (F) is orthogonal to d. So F · d = 0. That's correct. So we need to find t0 such that (M + t0 d) · d = 0 → M·d + t0 |d|^2 = 0 → t0 = - (M·d) / |d|^2.

Thus F = M - (M·d) / |d|^2 * d. Then B = 2F.

So B = 2M - 2 (M·d) / |d|^2 * d.

We can also express in terms of N and M. Since d = N - M.

(y0 - H_y)^2 - y0^2 = (n_x - m_x)^2 → (y0^2 - 2 y0 H_y + H_y^2) - y0^2 = -2 y0 H_y + H_y^2 = (n_x - m_x)^2.

Thus:

H_y^2 - 2 y0 H_y = (n_x - m_x)^2.   (Eq T)

We can also derive a similar equation from the radius expression: radius^2 = (n_x - m_x)^2 + y0^2. That's what we used.

Thus, I'll adopt the following coordinate system:

- Place A at the origin (0,0).

- The points E and F are the second intersections of line AP (x-axis) with Ω and Γ respectively. The condition |E - M| = r gives (e - m_x)^2 + m_y^2 = r^2. Since |M|^2 = r^2, we have:

(e - m_x)^2 + m_y^2 = m_x^2 + m_y^2 → e^2 - 2e m_x + m_x^2 + m_y^2 = m_x^2 + m_y^2 → e^2 - 2e m_x = 0 → e(e - 2 m_x) = 0. Since e ≠ 0 (E ≠ A), we get e = 2 m_x. So E = (2 m_x, 0). Good. Similarly, F = (2 n_x, 0). So indeed, E and F are determined by the x-coordinates of M and N.

Given the complexity, I'll attempt to carry out the algebra in a systematic way. I'll define variables:

p * C_x = (C_x^2 + C_y^2) / 2.  (EqC)

Similarly, p * D_x = (D_x^2 + D_y^2) / 2. (EqD)

Since p appears linearly, we can solve for p from EqC:

Now, also note that C_x could be zero. That would be a degenerate case where M and N have same x-coordinate? That's possible. (1) (m1 - n1) h_x + (m2 - n2) h_y = (m1 - n1) p.   (1)

We can solve for h_y. But also note that we could compute H using formula: In triangle with vertices P,M,N, the orthocenter H can be expressed as H = P + M + N - 2 O, but we can compute O using perpendicular bisectors. However, solving the linear system is straightforward.

Similarly, (2): (n1 - p) h_x + n2 h_y = (n1 - p) m1 + n2 m2.

We can solve for h_y. It might be messy but manageable.

[ a1, a1 p ]
[ a2, a2 m1 + b2 m2 ] 

Yes. So numerator N_y = a1 * (a2 m1 + b2 m2) - a2 * (a1 p). That's N_y = a1 a2 m1 + a1 b2 m2 - a1 a2 p.

Let's restate:

We have two circles Ω (M) and Γ (N) intersecting at A and B. Line MN meets Ω at C and Γ at D, with C-M-N-D order. P is circumcenter of ACD. H is orthocenter of PMN. AP meets Ω again at E (≠A) and Γ again at F (≠A). Prove that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to circumcircle of triangle BEF.

We'll set up coordinate system:

- Place A at the origin (0,0).
- Let the line AP be the x-axis. So P lies on the x-axis, P = (p,0) with p ≠ 0.
- Then E and F lie on the x-axis. As derived, with A=0, we have E = (2 m_x, 0) and F = (2 n_x, 0), where M = (m_x, m_y) and N = (n_x, n_y) are the centers of Ω and Γ.

Now, we have the following constraints:

1. |M| = r (since A is on Ω). So m_x^2 + m_y^2 = r^2.
2. |N| = R (since A is on Γ). So n_x^2 + n_y^2 = R^2.
3. The line MN intersects Ω at C and Γ at D with order C-M-N-D. We can compute C and D in terms of M,N,r,R. Since M and N are centers, and the line MN is the line through M and N. The point C is on Ω and on MN. Since A is on Ω, but A is not on MN generally. The intersection of MN with Ω gives two points: one on each side of M. The order C-M-N-D indicates that M is between C and N, and N is between M and D. So C is on the ray opposite to N from M. So C = M - (r / d) * (N - M), where d = MN. Similarly, D = N + (R / d) * (N - M). So these are formulas.

4. P is the circumcenter of triangle ACD, with A=0. So P is the unique point such that |P| = |P - C| = |P - D|. That yields:

  2 P·C = |C|^2,  2 P·D = |D|^2.   (1)

5. H is orthocenter of triangle PMN. So H satisfies:

   (H - M) · (N - P) = 0,   (H - N) · (M - P) = 0,   (H - P) · (M - N) = 0.   (2)

Now, our goal: Prove that the line through H parallel to AP (x-axis) is tangent to circle (BEF). Since AP is x-axis, the line through H parallel to AP is horizontal: y = H_y.

Now, B is the other intersection of circles. Since A=0 is an intersection, and MN is the perpendicular bisector of AB, we have B is the reflection of A across MN. So B = 2 * foot of perpendicular from A (origin) to line MN. Thus B = 2 Q, where Q is the foot.

Now, E = (2 m_x, 0). F = (2 n_x, 0). So the circle (BEF) has center O, with O_x = (2 m_x + 2 n_x)/2 = m_x + n_x. O_y is some value. So O = (m_x + n_x, y0). We can compute y0 from the condition that O is equidistant to B and E, or to B and F. Since O is on perpendicular bisector of EF (vertical line), and also on perpendicular bisector of BE.

Thus, y0 is given by:

y0 = \frac{ (m_x + n_x - B_x)^2 + B_y^2 - (n_x - m_x)^2 }{ 2 B_y }.

Now, we also have H_y. The tangency condition is:

(O_y - H_y)^2 = (n_x - m_x)^2 + O_y^2.   (T)

We can solve for h_y using Cramer's rule.

Let’s denote:

a = m1 - n1, b = m2 - n2.
c = n1 - p, d = n2.

Then system:

a h_x + b h_y = a p.      (1)
c h_x + d h_y = c m1 + d m2.   (2)

We want h_y.

Compute determinant Δ = a d - b c.

Then h_y = (a * (c m1 + d m2) - c * (a p)) / Δ = (a c (m1 - p) + a d m2) / Δ.

Thus:

h_y = \frac{ a (c (m1 - p) + d m2) ) }{ Δ }.

But a = m1 - n1, c = n1 - p, d = n2. So:

h_y = \frac{ (m1 - n1) [ (n1 - p)(m1 - p) + n2 m2 ] }{ (m1 - n1) n2 - (m2 - n2)(n1 - p) }.

O_x = \frac{ \begin{vmatrix} A_x^2+A_y^2 & A_y & 1 \\ B_x^2+B_y^2 & B_y & 1 \\ C_x^2+C_y^2 & C_y & 1 \end{vmatrix} }{ 2 \begin{vmatrix} A_x & A_y & 1 \\ B_x & B_y & 1 \\ C_x & C_y & 1 \end{vmatrix} } something like that. Let's derive. The circumcenter is the solution to:

2X·(B - A) = |B|^2 - |A|^2.
2X·(C - A) = |C|^2 - |A|^2.

So X is a vector. In matrix form, we have:

[ (B - A)^T; (C - A)^T ] X = [ (|B|^2 - |A|^2)/2; (|C|^2 - |A|^2)/2 ].

The solution can be expressed using Cramer's rule. That's the method we used. That's fine.

Then h_y = (A * (m1 C + m2 n2) - C * (p A)) / Δ = (A (m1 C + m2 n2) - p A C) / Δ = A (m1 C + m2 n2 - p C) / Δ = A (C (m1 - p) + m2 n2) / Δ.

Plug in M=0, so |A + t v|^2 = r^2. Since |A|^2 = r^2, we have:

|A|^2 + 2 t A·v + t^2 |v|^2 = r^2 → r^2 + 2 t A·v + t^2 |v|^2 = r^2 → 2 t A·v + t^2 |v|^2 = 0 → t (2 A·v + t |v|^2) = 0.

So t=0 (A) or t = -2 A·v / |v|^2. So t_E = -2 A·v / |v|^2.

Now, v = P - A. So A·v = A·(P - A) = A·P - |A|^2 = A·P - r^2. So t_E = -2 (A·P - r^2) / |v|^2 = 2 (r^2 - A·P) / |v|^2.

Thus, E = A + t_E v = A + \frac{2 (r^2 - A·P)}{|v|^2} (P - A).

Now, that's a linear combination. Similarly, for Γ: F = A + \frac{2 (R^2 - A·P)}{|v|^2} (P - A). (since |A - N|^2 = R^2, but careful: A·(P - A) appears in the formula with A - N. We'll derive later. But maybe it's simpler to compute using power relation.

But we can also compute A·P. In this coordinate system, P is (p_x, p_y). A = (x0, y0). So A·P = x0 p_x + y0 p_y. That's something.

Now, we also have that P is the circumcenter of ACD. That gives equations that might simplify A·P. Let's compute.

We have C = (-r,0), D = (d+R,0). Then P·C = -r p_x, P·D = (d+R) p_x. Also, |C|^2 = r^2, |D|^2 = (d+R)^2. The conditions from circumcenter:

2 P·C = |C|^2 → -2 r p_x = r^2 → p_x = -r/2.
2 P·D = |D|^2 → 2 (d+R) p_x = (d+R)^2 → p_x = (d+R)/2.

These are contradictory unless r = -(d+R) or something. But wait, we are using the condition that P is circumcenter of ACD. That gives:

|P - A|^2 = |P - C|^2 = |P - D|^2.

Thus:

|P|^2 - 2 P·A + |A|^2 = |P|^2 - 2 P·C + |C|^2 → -2 P·A + |A|^2 = -2 P·C + |C|^2 → 2 P·(C - A) = |C|^2 - |A|^2.
Similarly, 2 P·(D - A) = |D|^2 - |A|^2.

So we cannot simplify to P·C = |C|^2/2. That simplification occurred when A=0. So indeed, with A not at origin, the equations are more complex.

Thus, in the M,N on x-axis system, the expressions for P are not as simple as we might hope. However, we derived a nice expression for H_y: h_y = p_x (d - p_x) / p_y. That's derived from the fact that M and N are on x-axis. So that's good. But we need to compute p_y, which depends on A and C,D.

But we also can compute A·P maybe using that P is circumcenter. We have two equations:

2 P·(C - A) = |C|^2 - |A|^2.  (1)
2 P·(D - A) = |D|^2 - |A|^2.  (2)

We know |C|^2 = r^2, |D|^2 = (d+R)^2, |A|^2 = r^2. So |C|^2 - |A|^2 = 0. Thus, (1) gives:

2 P·(C - A) = 0 → P·(C - A) = 0. So P·C = P·A.

Similarly, (2) gives:

2 P·(D - A) = (d+R)^2 - r^2. But we also know that A lies on Γ, so (A - N)^2 = R^2 → (x0 - d)^2 + y0^2 = R^2. And |N|^2 = d^2. So (d+R)^2 - r^2 is something like? Not obviously zero. So we have:

P·(D - A) = \frac{ (d+R)^2 - r^2 }{2}.   (2')

Now, also P·C = P·A. That's interesting.

Since C = (-r,0), P·C = -r p_x. So -r p_x = P·A. Thus, P·A = -r p_x. So A·P = -r p_x. That's a relation.

Now, we also have from the definition of P as circumcenter: |P - A|^2 = |P - C|^2. That gave earlier equation with p_y. But we can also use the relation P·A = -r p_x. That might simplify the expression for t_E and s_F.

Now, also we can find p_y from the equation |P - A|^2 = |P - C|^2. That yields:

(p_x - x0)^2 + (p_y - y0)^2 = (p_x + r)^2 + p_y^2.

Simplify: (p_x - x0)^2 + p_y^2 - 2 p_y y0 + y0^2 = (p_x + r)^2 + p_y^2 → cancel p_y^2 → (p_x - x0)^2 - 2 p_y y0 + y0^2 = (p_x + r)^2.

Thus, -2 p_y y0 = (p_x + r)^2 - (p_x - x0)^2 - y0^2.

So 2 p_y y0 = (p_x - x0)^2 + y0^2 - (p_x + r)^2.

Now, we also know that A·P = -r p_x. So x0 p_x + y0 p_y = -r p_x. That gives:

y0 p_y = -r p_x - x0 p_x = -p_x (r + x0). So p_y = -p_x (r + x0) / y0.

But we also have from the equation above an expression for p_y. That should be consistent. Let's compute p_y from that equation:

p_y = \frac{ (p_x - x0)^2 + y0^2 - (p_x + r)^2 }{ 2 y0 }.

Simplify numerator: (p_x^2 - 2 p_x x0 + x0^2) + y0^2 - (p_x^2 + 2 r p_x + r^2) = -2 p_x x0 + x0^2 + y0^2 - 2 r p_x - r^2. But x0^2 + y0^2 = r^2. So numerator = -2 p_x x0 + r^2 - 2 r p_x - r^2 = -2 p_x x0 - 2 r p_x = -2 p_x (x0 + r). So

p_y = \frac{ -2 p_x (x0 + r) }{ 2 y0 } = - \frac{ p_x (x0 + r) }{ y0 }.

That's consistent with the relation from A·P = -r p_x? Let's check: From A·P = x0 p_x + y0 p_y = -r p_x → y0 p_y = -p_x (r + x0). So p_y = -p_x (r + x0) / y0. Yes, that's exactly the same. So indeed, the relation A·P = -r p_x follows from P·C = P·A (which we derived from the circumcenter condition). So we have that.

Now, we also have from the other equation (2') that P·(D - A) = something. That might relate p_x, p_y, etc. But maybe we can find p_y directly from the expression we derived, which is p_y = -p_x (x0 + r) / y0. That's fine.

Now, also we can compute H_y = p_x (d - p_x) / p_y. So H_y = p_x (d - p_x) / ( - p_x (x0 + r) / y0 ) = - (d - p_x) y0 / (x0 + r). That's a nice simplification! Because p_x cancels. So H_y = - \frac{ (d - p_x) y0 }{ x0 + r }.

Now, p_x = (d + R - r)/2. So d - p_x = d - (d + R - r)/2 = (2d - d - R + r)/2 = (d - R + r)/2. So H_y = - \frac{ (d - R + r) y0 }{ 2 (x0 + r) }.

That's what we have.

- A = (0,0).

- M = (a,b), N = (c,d).

Now, we have the circumcenter condition for ACD:

2 P·C = |C|^2, 2 P·D = |D|^2. Since P is on x-axis, P·C = p C_x, P·D = p D_x. So:

p = |C|^2 / (2 C_x) = |D|^2 / (2 D_x).   (2)

Now, compute C and D in terms of M,N. Let's denote:

v = N - M = (c - a, d - b). Then d = |v| = sqrt((c-a)^2 + (d-b)^2). Then:

C = M - (r / d) v. So C = (a,b) - (r/d) (c-a, d-b) = (a - (r/d)(c-a), b - (r/d)(d-b)). Similarly,

D = N + (R / d) v = (c,d) + (R/d) (c-a, d-b) = (c + (R/d)(c-a), d + (R/d)(d-b)).

Thus, C_x = a - (r/d)(c-a). D_x = c + (R/d)(c-a).

Now, |C|^2 = (a - (r/d)(c-a))^2 + (b - (r/d)(d-b))^2. That's messy. However, note that we also have the relations from A being on Ω and Γ: a^2 + b^2 = r^2, (c - d? Wait A is origin, so |M|^2 = r^2, |N|^2 = R^2. So a^2 + b^2 = r^2, c^2 + d^2 = R^2. Also, we have the distance between M and N: d^2 = (c-a)^2 + (d-b)^2. So we have three squared distances.

We can try to compute |C|^2 in a simpler form. Since C = M - (r/d) v, we have:

|C|^2 = |M|^2 - 2 (r/d) M·v + (r^2/d^2) |v|^2 = r^2 - 2 (r/d) M·v + (r^2/d^2) d^2 = r^2 - 2 (r/d) M·v + r^2 = 2 r^2 - 2 (r/d) M·v.

Thus, |C|^2 = 2 r^2 - \frac{2 r}{d} (M·v). That's nice. Similarly, for D: D = N + (R/d) v. So |D|^2 = |N|^2 + 2 (R/d) N·v + (R^2/d^2) |v|^2 = R^2 + 2 (R/d) N·v + (R^2/d^2) d^2 = R^2 + 2 (R/d) N·v + R^2 = 2 R^2 + 2 (R/d) N·v.

So |D|^2 = 2 R^2 + \frac{2 R}{d} (N·v). Good.

Now, also compute C_x. C_x = a - (r/d)(c-a). That's a linear combination.

Similarly, from D: p = \frac{ R^2 + \frac{R}{d} (N·v) }{ c + \frac{R}{d}(c-a) }.

Thus, we have two expressions for p. They must be consistent. This gives a relation between a,b,c,d,r,R. That's okay.

These are three linear equations. The solution can be obtained by solving a 2x2 system. That's what we did. So it's okay.

However, there is a known fact: If a point lies on a line that is tangent to a circle, then the distance from the point to the center is the length of the tangent? No.

Actually, for a point P outside a circle, the length of the tangent segment from P to the circle is sqrt(Power). But here H is on the tangent line, but not necessarily the point of tangency. So we cannot use that.

Now, consider the orthocenter H of triangle PMN. Since MP ⟂ AC and NP ⟂ AD. So the lines MP and NP are perpendicular to AC and AD respectively. Also, AB ⟂ MN. So we have many perpendicularities.

But we also have that D is on Γ and on line MN. So maybe we can consider the spiral similarity at A that sends M to N. Then the image of the line MN (which passes through M and N) is the line passing through N and something? Actually, since A is the center, the image of a point X on MN (not A) is A + k Rθ (X - A). The line MN (not passing through A) will map to a line that is not MN generally. That seems messy.

Another idea: Use the fact that H is the orthocenter, so the reflection of H across MN lies on the circumcircle of PMN. That reflection, call H', is such that H' is on circumcircle. Since M and N are on MN, and P is some point. The circumcircle of PMN is some circle. Maybe we can find that H' is exactly the point A? Or something like that. Hmm.

Simplify: (p_x - x0)^2 + p_y^2 - 2 p_y y0 + y0^2 = (p_x + r)^2 + p_y^2 → cancel p_y^2 → (p_x - x0)^2 - 2 p_y y0 + y0^2 = (p_x + r)^2.

Thus, -2 p_y y0 = (p_x + r)^2 - (p_x - x0)^2 - y0^2.

So 2 p_y y0 = (p_x - x0)^2 + y0^2 - (p_x + r)^2.
