## Solution
We present a rigorous proof using coordinate geometry.  
Let the two circles be $\Omega$ (center $M$, radius $r$) and $\Gamma$ (center $N$, radius $R$) with $r<R$.  
They intersect at two distinct points $A$ and $B$ and the line $MN$ meets $\Omega$ at $C$ and $\Gamma$ at $D$ such that $C,M,N,D$ lie on $MN$ in that order.  
Denote $P$ the circumcenter of $\triangle ACD$ and $H$ the orthocenter of $\triangle PMN$.  
Line $AP$ meets $\Omega$ again at $E\neq A$ and $\Gamma$ again at $F\neq A$.  
We shall prove that the line through $H$ parallel to $AP$ is tangent to the circumcircle of $\triangle BEF$.

---

### 1.  A convenient coordinate system
Place $A$ at the origin and let $AP$ be the $x$‑axis.  
Because $AP$ is a line through $A$, we may rotate the whole figure so that $AP$ becomes horizontal.  
After this rigid motion we have  

\[
A=(0,0),\qquad P=(p,0)\quad(p\neq0).
\]

The points $E$ and $F$ lie on $AP$, hence they have coordinates  

\[
E=(e,0),\qquad F=(f,0).
\]

The centers $M$ and $N$ of $\Omega$ and $\Gamma$ are not on $AP$ in general; they satisfy  

\[
|M|=r,\qquad |N|=R
\]

because $A$ belongs to both circles.  Write  

\[
M=(a,b),\qquad N=(c,d),\qquad a^2+b^2=r^2,\quad c^2+d^2=R^2.
\]

---

### 2.  Express $E$ and $F$ via $M$ and $N$
Let $d_{MN}=MN$.  The line $MN$ (the line of centers) meets $\Omega$ at $C$ and $\Gamma$ at $D$ with the order $C\!-\!M\!-\!N\!-\!D$.  
Consequently $C$ is on $\Omega$ and on $MN$ on the side opposite $N$, $D$ is on $\Gamma$ and on $MN$ on the side opposite $M$.  
Parametrising $MN$ by $M+t\,(N-M)$ we obtain  

\[
C = M - \frac{r}{d_{MN}}\,(N-M),\qquad 
D = N + \frac{R}{d_{MN}}\,(N-M). \tag{1}
\]

Now $AP$ is the $x$‑axis, so $E$ and $F$ are the second intersections of the $x$‑axis with $\Omega$ and $\Gamma$.  
For a circle centered at $M$ with radius $r$, a line through $A$ (the origin) meets it again at $E$.  Because $A$ is on $\Omega$, the second intersection $E$ satisfies  

\[
|E-M|^2 = r^2,\qquad |A-M|^2 = r^2.
\]

Writing $E=(e,0)$ we obtain  

\[
(e-a)^2 + b^2 = a^2+b^2 \;\Longrightarrow\; e^2-2ea=0 \;\Longrightarrow\; e(e-2a)=0.
\]

Since $E\neq A$, $e\neq0$, hence $e=2a$.  Similarly, using $\Gamma$ we obtain $f=2c$.  Thus  

\[
E=(2a,0),\qquad F=(2c,0). \tag{2}
\]

---

### 3.  Determine $P$ (circumcenter of $\triangle ACD$)
Because $A$ is the origin, $P$ must satisfy  

\[
|P|^2 = |P-C|^2 = |P-D|^2.
\]

The equalities give the linear equations  

\[
2\,P\!\cdot\!C = |C|^2,\qquad 2\,P\!\cdot\!D = |D|^2. \tag{3}
\]

Since $P=(p,0)$ lies on the $x$‑axis, we have  

\[
p\,C_x = \frac{|C|^2}{2},\qquad p\,D_x = \frac{|D|^2}{2}. \tag{4}
\]

Now compute $C$ and $D$ in terms of $M,N$ using (1).  Let  

\[
\mathbf{v}=N-M=(c-a,\;d-b),\qquad \ell = |\mathbf{v}| = d_{MN}.
\]

Then  

\[
C = M - \frac{r}{\ell}\,\mathbf{v},\qquad 
D = N + \frac{R}{\ell}\,\mathbf{v}.
\]

A direct computation yields  

\[
|C|^2 = |M|^2 - \frac{2r}{\ell}\,M\!\cdot\!\mathbf{v} + \frac{r^2}{\ell^2}|\mathbf{v}|^2
      = r^2 - \frac{2r}{\ell}\,M\!\cdot\!\mathbf{v} + r^2
      = 2r^2 - \frac{2r}{\ell}\,M\!\cdot\!\mathbf{v},
\]  

\[
|D|^2 = |N|^2 + \frac{2R}{\ell}\,N\!\cdot\!\mathbf{v} + \frac{R^2}{\ell^2}|\mathbf{v}|^2
      = R^2 + \frac{2R}{\ell}\,N\!\cdot\!\mathbf{v} + R^2
      = 2R^2 + \frac{2R}{\ell}\,N\!\cdot\!\mathbf{v}. \tag{5}
\]

Also  

\[
C_x = a - \frac{r}{\ell}\,(c-a),\qquad 
D_x = c + \frac{R}{\ell}\,(c-a). \tag{6}
\]

From (4) we obtain two expressions for $p$:

\[
p = \frac{|C|^2}{2\,C_x} = \frac{2r^2 - \frac{2r}{\ell}\,M\!\cdot\!\mathbf{v}}{2\bigl(a-\frac{r}{\ell}(c-a)\bigr)}
   = \frac{r^2 - \frac{r}{\ell}\,M\!\cdot\!\mathbf{v}}{a - \frac{r}{\ell}(c-a)},
\]  

\[
p = \frac{|D|^2}{2\,D_x} = \frac{2R^2 + \frac{2R}{\ell}\,N\!\cdot\!\mathbf{v}}{2\bigl(c+\frac{R}{\ell}(c-a)\bigr)}
   = \frac{R^2 + \frac{R}{\ell}\,N\!\cdot\!\mathbf{v}}{c + \frac{R}{\ell}(c-a)}. \tag{7}
\]

Both formulas must agree; the consistency will be used later.

---

### 4.  Compute $H$ (orthocenter of $\triangle PMN$)
In our coordinate system $P=(p,0)$, $M=(a,b)$, $N=(c,d)$.  
The orthocenter $H$ is the unique point such that  

\[
(H-P)\!\cdot\!(M-N)=0,\qquad (H-M)\!\cdot\!(N-P)=0,\qquad (H-N)\!\cdot\!(M-P)=0.
\]

The first condition gives  

\[
(H-P)\!\cdot\!(M-N)=0 \;\Longrightarrow\; H\!\cdot\!(M-N) = P\!\cdot\!(M-N) = p\,(a-c). \tag{8}
\]

The second condition gives  

\[
(H-M)\!\cdot\!(N-P)=0 \;\Longrightarrow\; H\!\cdot\!(N-P) = M\!\cdot\!(N-P). \tag{9}
\]

Since $P$ is on the $x$‑axis, we have $N-P=(c-p,\;d)$.  
We solve (8) and (9) for $H$.  Let $H=(h_x,h_y)$.  Then  

\[
\begin{aligned}
&(a-c)\,h_x + (b-d)\,h_y = p\,(a-c), \\[2mm]
&(c-p)\,h_x + d\,h_y = a\,(c-p) + b\,d.
\end{aligned}
\]

Solving (e.g. by Cramer’s rule) yields  

\[
h_y = \frac{(a-c)\bigl[(c-p)(a-p)+b d\bigr]}{(a-c)d - (b-d)(c-p)}. \tag{10}
\]

Now use the fact that $A$ lies on $\Omega$ and $\Gamma$; $a^2+b^2=r^2$, $c^2+d^2=R^2$.  
Also note that $b d$ appears.  It will be convenient to express $b d$ as $M\!\cdot\!N - a c$.  
But we can also simplify $h_y$ by noticing that $P$ is the circumcenter of $ACD$, so (4) gives relations linking $p$ to $C_x$ and $D_x$.  We will not need the full explicit $h_y$ because later we will find a compact expression for $h_y$ using $p$ and the coordinates of $M,N$ derived from the geometry of $\Omega$ and $\Gamma$ (see Section 5).

---

### 5.  Express $h_y$ in a compact form
From (4) we have $p\,C_x = \frac{|C|^2}{2}$ and $p\,D_x = \frac{|D|^2}{2}$.  Because $C$ and $D$ are on $MN$ we can also use the relation $P\!\cdot\!C = P\!\cdot\!A + \frac{|C|^2-|A|^2}{2}$ etc.  But a more direct way is to use the perpendicular bisector of $CD$.  Since $C$ and $D$ lie on $MN$ and $A$ is the origin, we already have $P_x = p$ (because $P$ is on the $x$‑axis) and also from $|P-C|^2=|P-A|^2$ we derived  

\[
p\,C_x = \frac{|C|^2}{2}. \tag{11}
\]

But we can also derive an expression for $p$ from the coordinates of $M,N$ without radicals.  Let us compute $C_x$ and $|C|^2$ explicitly in terms of $a,b,c,d,\ell$ and then $p$.  Using (1) and (5) we have  

\[
\begin{aligned}
C_x &= a - \frac{r}{\ell}\,(c-a),\\
|C|^2 &= 2r^2 - \frac{2r}{\ell}\,M\!\cdot\!\mathbf{v},\qquad M\!\cdot\!\mathbf{v}=a(c-a)+b(d-b)= (a c+b d)-(a^2+b^2) = M\!\cdot\!N - r^2.
\end{aligned}
\]

Thus  

\[
p = \frac{r^2 - \frac{r}{\ell}\,(M\!\cdot\!N - r^2)}{a - \frac{r}{\ell}\,(c-a)}. \tag{12}
\]

Similarly, from $D$ we have  

\[
p = \frac{R^2 + \frac{R}{\ell}\,(R^2 - M\!\cdot\!N)}{c + \frac{R}{\ell}\,(c-a)}. \tag{13}
\]

Now the orthocenter $H$ of $\triangle PMN$ satisfies $H\cdot(M-N) = p\,(a-c)$.  Because $P$ is on the $x$‑axis and $M,N$ are arbitrary, we can also compute $h_y$ via vector formulas.  However we can avoid the messy determinant by noticing that the line $MN$ is the perpendicular bisector of $AB$ (since $A$ is an intersection point).  Indeed, $M$ and $N$ are centers of $\Omega$ and $\Gamma$, so $MA = MC$ and $NA = NB$; thus $MN$ is the perpendicular bisector of $AB$.  Because $A$ is the origin, $B$ is the reflection of $A$ across $MN$, i.e. $B = 2\,Q$ where $Q$ is the foot of the perpendicular from $A$ to $MN$.  

Now we consider the circle through $B,E,F$ (the circumcircle of $\triangle BEF$).  Its center $O$ lies on the perpendicular bisector of $EF$.  Since $E=(2a,0)$ and $F=(2c,0)$, the perpendicular bisector of $EF$ is the vertical line $x = a + c$.  Therefore $O = (a+c,\; y_0)$ for some $y_0$.

The condition $|O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2$ yields  

\[
(a+c - B_x)^2 + (y_0 - B_y)^2 = (a+c - 2a)^2 + y_0^2 = (c-a)^2 + y_0^2.
\]

Cancelling $y_0^2$ and expanding gives  

\[
(a+c - B_x)^2 - 2\,y_0\,B_y + B_y^2 = (c-a)^2.
\]

Thus  

\[
2\,y_0\,B_y = (a+c - B_x)^2 + B_y^2 - (c-a)^2. \tag{14}
\]

We will compute $B_x,B_y$ in terms of $M,N$ using the reflection formula.  The line $MN$ has direction $\mathbf{v}=N-M$; the foot $Q$ of the perpendicular from the origin to $MN$ is  

\[
Q = M - \frac{M\!\cdot\!\mathbf{v}}{|\mathbf{v}|^2}\,\mathbf{v}
    = \bigl(a,b\bigr) - \frac{a(c-a)+b(d-b)}{(c-a)^2+(d-b)^2}\,\bigl(c-a,\;d-b\bigr). \tag{15}
\]

Hence $B = 2Q$, so  

\[
B_x = 2a - \frac{2\,(a(c-a)+b(d-b))}{|\mathbf{v}|^2}\,(c-a),\qquad
B_y = 2b - \frac{2\,(a(c-a)+b(d-b))}{|\mathbf{v}|^2}\,(d-b). \tag{16}
\]

Now we also have the orthocenter $H$ of $\triangle PMN$.  As derived, $H$ satisfies $H\cdot(M-N) = p\,(a-c)$.  Because $P$ is on the $x$‑axis, we can compute $h_y$ explicitly by solving the two linear equations (8) and (9).  However a much simpler expression emerges if we also use the fact that $P$ is the circumcenter of $ACD$ and $A$ is the origin.  From $|P-C|^2 = |P-A|^2$ we already obtained  

\[
p\,C_x = \frac{|C|^2}{2}. \tag{17}
\]

But also from $|P-D|^2 = |P-A|^2$ we obtain  

\[
p\,D_x = \frac{|D|^2}{2}. \tag{18}
\]

Now recall that $C$ and $D$ lie on $MN$ and are given by (1).  Let us compute $C_x$ and $D_x$ in terms of $a,b,c,d$.  We have  

\[
C_x = a - \frac{r}{\ell}\,(c-a),\qquad D_x = c + \frac{R}{\ell}\,(c-a).
\]

Plugging these into (17) and (18) gives  

\[
p = \frac{|C|^2}{2\,C_x} = \frac{2r^2 - \frac{2r}{\ell}\,(M\!\cdot\!N - r^2)}{2\bigl(a - \frac{r}{\ell}(c-a)\bigr)} 
   = \frac{r^2 - \frac{r}{\ell}\,(M\!\cdot\!N - r^2)}{a - \frac{r}{\ell}(c-a)}. \tag{19}
\]

Now we also have $h_y$ from (10).  Let us attempt to simplify $h_y$ using the relations involving $p$.  From (10) we see that $h_y$ is expressed as a rational function of $a,b,c,d,p$.  The numerator involves $b d$, which is $M\!\cdot\!N - a c$.  The denominator involves $d$ and $b$.  Perhaps we can relate $h_y$ to $p$ and the coordinates of $M,N$ in a simple way.

Observation: In $\triangle PMN$, the altitude from $P$ is perpendicular to $MN$.  Since $P$ lies on the $x$‑axis, the line through $P$ perpendicular to $MN$ is the line $x = p$ (if $MN$ is not vertical).  But $MN$ is not necessarily horizontal; the altitude from $P$ is not horizontal in general.  However we have a compact expression for $h_y$ if we also consider the fact that $H$ is the orthocenter and we know $P$, $M$, $N$.  Another approach is to use vector formulas for orthocenter in terms of coordinates.  But maybe we can find a relationship between $h_y$ and $y_0$ that does not require solving for $p$ explicitly, by using the fact that $P$ is circumcenter of $ACD$ and $A$ is origin, which gives $p$ expressed as something like $\frac{r^2 - \frac{r}{\ell} (M\!\cdot\!N - r^2)}{a - \frac{r}{\ell}(c-a)}$.

Let's attempt to compute $h_y$ directly from (10) and then substitute $p$ from (19).  That will yield an expression.  Then we will also compute $y_0$ from (14) and then check the tangency condition $H_y^2 - 2 H_y y_0 = (c-a)^2$.  This seems like a heavy algebraic undertaking, but with systematic simplifications it is doable.  However, to keep the solution manageable, we can adopt a different coordinate choice that makes the algebra simpler.  We'll do that now.

---

### 6.  A better coordinate choice
Place $A$ at the origin and let $AP$ be the $x$‑axis (as before).  This gives $E = (2a,0)$ and $F = (2c,0)$ with $M=(a,b)$, $N=(c,d)$.  
Now we also use the fact that $MN$ is the perpendicular bisector of $AB$ (since $A$ is an intersection).  Hence $B$ is the reflection of $A$ across $MN$, i.e. $B = 2Q$ where $Q$ is the foot of the perpendicular from $A$ to $MN$.  So $B_x$ and $B_y$ are as in (16).

Now compute $H$, the orthocenter of $\triangle PMN$.  Because $P$ is on the $x$‑axis, we can find $H$ by solving  

\[
\begin{cases}
(H - P)\cdot (M - N) = 0,\\
(H - M)\cdot (N - P) = 0.
\end{cases}
\]

The first gives  

\[
(a-c)\,h_x + (b-d)\,h_y = p\,(a-c). \tag{8}
\]

The second gives  

\[
(c-p)\,h_x + d\,h_y = a\,(c-p) + b\,d. \tag{9}
\]

Solving these two linear equations yields $h_y$ as in (10).  But we can also compute $h_y$ using a vector formula for orthocenter.  There is a known expression:  

\[
H = \frac{ \begin{vmatrix} a^2+b^2 & b & 1 \\ c^2+d^2 & d & 1 \\ p^2+0^2 & 0 & 1 \end{vmatrix} }{ \begin{vmatrix} a & b & 1 \\ c & d & 1 \\ p & 0 & 1 \end{vmatrix} } \text{ something?}
\]

Actually that's for circumcenter.  I recall a formula for orthocenter using coordinates:  

\[
H = \frac{ \begin{vmatrix} x_1^2+y_1^2 & y_1 & 1 \\ x_2^2+y_2^2 & y_2 & 1 \\ x_3^2+y_3^2 & y_3 & 1 \end{vmatrix} }{ \begin{vmatrix} x_1 & y_1 & 1 \\ x_2 & y_2 & 1 \\ x_3 & y_3 & 1 \end{vmatrix} } \cdot \text{ something?} 
\]

But that's for circumcenter.  For orthocenter, it's similar but with the signs of $y$ terms changed?  Actually the formula for circumcenter is  

\[
O_x = \frac{ \begin{vmatrix} x_1^2+y_1^2 & y_1 & 1 \\ x_2^2+y_2^2 & y_2 & 1 \\ x_3^2+y_3^2 & y_3 & 1 \end{vmatrix} }{ 2 \begin{vmatrix} x_1 & y_1 & 1 \\ x_2 & y_2 & 1 \\ x_3 & y_3 & 1 \end{vmatrix} } \cdot \text{?}
\]

I'm mixing.  Let's derive properly.

The circumcenter $O$ satisfies  

\[
|O - A|^2 = |O - B|^2 \;\Longrightarrow\; 2O\cdot(B-A) = |B|^2 - |A|^2,
\]  

and similarly $2O\cdot(C-A) = |C|^2 - |A|^2$.  

So $O$ is a solution to a $2\times2$ linear system.  The determinant of the coefficient matrix is $2\cdot (B-A) \times (C-A
